ok so lets zoom in on the full api. but lets just talk about it right now, what is our current implementation ?
assistant
Right now `main` does NOT use the full API. `main`'s Allstate `fetchDocuments` is pure UI nav: click Policies dropdown, click "Documents for ... policy", read the table anchors, click each title to trigger a popup+download, then pull bytes from Browserbase's download storage. That's the ~12s path.

The full-API version only exists on the branch `allstate-api-experiment` (not merged). And honestly what we have committed there is the *recipe proven out*, plus the discovery script (`scripts/allstate-docsniff.ts`). Let me lay out the full-API flow as it stands:

**The 4 calls (all via `page.request`, in-browser, authed by session cookies):**

1. `GET /api/secured/GetUserData` → grab `policies[0].policyImage.number` (the policy number).
2. `GET /api/secured/document/GetDocumentsForPolicies` → the **context primer**. Returns an empty body, but without it step 3 comes back empty. This was the blocker we cracked.
3. `POST /api/secured/document/GetPolicySpecificDocsList` with body `{policyNumber, contentId:null, yearFilter}` → `[{title, contentId, docYear}]`, the doc list.
4. `POST /api/secured/document/GetUdpRetrieveDocument` with `{policyNumber, contentId, yearFilter}` per doc → `documentData.{data(base64), mimeType, fileName}`. Fire these in parallel.

**The three things that made it work:**
- CSRF header `x-xsrf-token` must be `decodeURIComponent(XSRF-TOKEN cookie)`, not the raw cookie.
- POST body must be `JSON.stringify(...)`, not a Playwright `data` object (else server sees null body).
- Required headers: `app-name: MYA`, `x-efm: true`, `content-type: application/json` + the decoded token.

**What we proved:** not anti-bot gated (Akamai `_abck` stays `-1` but `/api/secured` returns 200), ~6-7s reliable.

Two things to flag about "current state" before we go further:

1. **It's a branch, and I should confirm what the actual `allstate.ts` on that branch looks like** vs just the recipe in my notes. Want me to check out that branch's `allstate.ts`?
2. **The source-IP caveat is still unverified.** `page.request` on a Browserbase CDP browser probably fires from *our Node process IP*, not through the residential proxy. Allstate accepted it in testing because the endpoints check session+CSRF, not IP, but we never confirmed it on prod.

What angle do you want to dig into. The branch code, the IP caveat, or whether it goes on main?

import { CarrierError, InvalidMfaError } from "../errors.js";
import { validateDocuments } from "../documents.js";
import { BrowserbaseSession, step, requireSession } from "../browserbase.js";

const LOGIN_URL =
    process.env.ALLSTATE_LOGIN_URL ?? "https://myaccountrwd.allstate.com/anon/account/login";
const STEP_TIMEOUT = 30_000;

// Real Allstate portal automation. Selectors confirmed against the live login:
// email tab #UserIDdisplay, inputs #emailAddress + visible password, submit
// button[name=frmButton]:visible. Login lands on /anon/verification (MFA).
// Shared Browserbase plumbing (session + downloads) lives in BrowserbaseSession.
export class AllstateCarrier implements Carrier {
    readonly name = "allstate";
    private session?: BrowserbaseSession;

    // Open the browser and load the login form. No credentials needed, so this
    // can run ahead of login() to pre-warm while the user is still typing.
    async prepare(): Promise<void> {
        this.session = await BrowserbaseSession.open();

        // Kill CSS animations/transitions so Playwright's click "stability" waits

assistant
Got it. Here's the exact map of what's on `allstate-api-experiment`.

The whole file is identical to `main` **except `fetchDocuments`**. `prepare` / `login` / `submitMfa` are the same UI automation (load form, type creds, race MFA-vs-dashboard, SMS code). The browser still logs in like a human. The divergence is only *how it pulls the docs* once logged in.

## What `fetchDocuments` does on the branch

It throws away the UI nav entirely and talks to Allstate's internal JSON API directly, using `page.request` (Playwright's in-browser HTTP client, so it rides the same logged-in session cookies).

**Step 0 — build auth headers**
```
xsrfCookie = [REDACTED]
headers = {
  x-xsrf-token: [REDACTED],   // the decode is the whole trick
  app-name: MYA,
  x-efm: true,
  content-type: application/json,
  accept: application/json...,
  referer: .../secured/documents/policy-documents
}
```

**Step 1 — who am I / which policy** `GET /api/secured/GetUserData` → reads `policies[0].policyImage.number`. If missing, throws `CarrierError`. Also grabs `year = current year`.

**Step 2 — prime the context** `GET /api/secured/document/GetDocumentsForPolicies`. Response is ignored. It exists purely to make the server scope the session to this policy's docs. Skip it and step 3 returns empty.

**Step 3 — list the docs** `POST /api/secured/document/GetPolicySpecificDocsList` with `JSON.stringify({policyNumber, contentId:null, yearFilter:year})` → reads `policySpecificDocumentsList[]`, each `{title, contentId, docYear}`.

**Step 4 — pull each PDF in parallel** for each item, `POST /api/secured/document/GetUdpRetrieveDocument` with `{policyNumber, contentId, yearFilter}` → reads `documentData.{data, mimeType}`. Maps to `{name: title, contentType: mimeType, bytes: data}`. `Promise.all` so they overlap.

Then the same `validateDocuments(this.name, docs)` as main (throws if zero).

## How this differs from main's path

| | main (UI) | branch (API) |
|---|---|---|
| find docs | click Policies → Documents, scrape `<table a>` | `GetUserData` + list endpoint |
| download | click each title → popup → download into Browserbase storage → poll Downloads API → fetch base64 | one POST per doc, base64 in the JSON response |
| bytes source | Browserbase Downloads API | `documentData.data` |
| browser steps after login | ~6 interactions | zero (no clicks, no nav) |
| time | ~12s | ~6-7s |

## Two things worth your eye

1. **`bytes` is whatever `documentData.data` is** — base64 from the API. The UI path also ends up base64 (from the Downloads API), so the frontend's blob-URL render should be consistent, but I have not byte-for-byte confirmed the two produce the same `Document.bytes` shape. Worth a check before merging.

2. **`page.request` source IP.** This is the unverified caveat. `page.request` for a CDP/Browserbase browser most likely sends from *our Fly Node process IP*, not through the residential proxy the real browser uses. It worked in testing because these endpoints gate on session+CSRF not IP, but it means the API calls have a different network fingerprint than the login did. We never confirmed behavior on prod.

So: login is human-and-proxied, doc fetch is a direct API hit possibly from a different IP. That's the picture. Where do you want to push.
user
ok so how did we find their internal api docs?
assistant
No magic, no leaked docs. We watched the real site talk to itself. The method, in order:

**1. Network sniffing of the live UI.** The script `scripts/allstate-docsniff.ts` logs in through the normal UI on Browserbase, then attaches `page.on("request")` / `page.on("response")` listeners and flips `capture = true` right before the doc phase. As we clicked Policies → Documents → a title, the Angular SPA fired its own XHRs and we logged every `allstate.com` request that wasn't noise. The `NOISE` regex filters out the junk (datadog, demdex, omtrdc, fonts, css, js, images) so only the real API calls surface. That's how `GetUserData`, `GetPolicySpecificDocsList`, `GetUdpRetrieveDocument` showed up, with their methods, URLs, POST bodies, and headers.

**2. Captured the request shapes.** For the calls we cared about it printed the POST body (`LIST postData`) and the non-cookie headers, so we learned the body schema (`{policyNumber, contentId, yearFilter}`) and the required headers (`app-name: MYA`, `x-efm: true`, the xsrf token).

**3. Replay test in-browser.** The script doesn't just observe, it re-fires the captured request via `page.request.post(...)` to prove we could call the endpoint ourselves with the same session, not just watch the app do it. That's the section that replays `GetUdpRetrieveDocument` and checks the status + content-type of the response.

**4. Then we hit walls and probed each one.** The replay kept 500ing. Three specific probes cracked it:
- **CSRF probe** — the script fires the list call with three token variants (raw cookie, `decodeURIComponent(cookie)`, the app's own header token) and prints which returns 200. That's how we learned the raw URL-encoded cookie 500s and the decoded value works. The script even logs `decodeURIComponent(cookie) === appHeader: true` to confirm it matches what Angular sends.
- **Null body** — list came back with `policyNumber:null` until we switched from a Playwright `data` object to `JSON.stringify(...)`.
- **Empty list cold** — list returned empty until we noticed the app always GETs `GetDocumentsForPolicies` first. That was the context-primer insight.

**5. Research agents** corroborated the primer step and the "not anti-bot" read (the `_abck=-1` observation) so we weren't guessing.

So the short version: it's their own SPA's traffic, captured with Playwright request/response hooks, then replayed and debugged with targeted probes. Nothing came from documentation, it was all observed off the wire from the authenticated session.

Honest answer: [REDACTED] don't have that split actually logged, so I shouldn't quote you a number I didn't measure. Here's what we do and don't know.

The branch logs **two** timings inside `fetchDocuments`:
- `api-list` = `GetUserData` + the `GetDocumentsForPolicies` primer + `GetPolicySpecificDocsList` (three sequential round trips)
- `api-docs` = the parallel `GetUdpRetrieveDocument` byte pulls

What's recorded in `latency.md` is only the **total doc stage ~6-7s** for the full-API path. We never wrote down the api-list vs api-docs breakdown.

My estimate, not a measurement: the **actual byte fetching** (`api-docs`, the parallel POSTs) is probably the *small* part, roughly 1-2s, because they run in parallel and the analogous UI `fetch-bytes` step was ~0.7s. The bulk of the ~6-7s is more likely the **three sequential calls in `api-list`** (each is a server round trip, and the primer specifically waits on Allstate's backend to scope the policy context).

If that estimate holds, the obvious next lever is the one already noted in `latency.md`: `GetUserData` and the `GetDocumentsForPolicies` primer don't depend on each other, so they could fire in parallel instead of sequentially.

A custom `fetch` function can be provided during client instantiation to inspect or modify requests and responses. The SDK also automatically logs all requests and responses if the `DEBUG=true` environment variable is set, though this logging is intended for debugging and may change.

### README.md > Advanced Usage > Making custom/undocumented requests

Source: https://github.com/browserbase/sdk-node/blob/main/README.md

For undocumented endpoints, parameters, or response properties, the library can still be used. Undocumented endpoints can be accessed using `client.get`, `client.post`, etc., respecting client options like retries. Undocumented request parameters can be included by using `// @ts-expect-error` on the parameter, and they will be sent as-is. For GET requests, extra parameters go in the query; for others, they go in the body. Undocumented response properties can be accessed similarly with `// @ts-expect-error` or by casting.

Got clean numbers. One thing first, then the data.

## ⚠️ Security: your Browserbase API key leaked into the output
`DEBUG=true` makes the Browserbase SDK log every request including the `x-bb-api-key` header, so `bb_live_[REDACTED]` is now in plaintext in the task output file and this transcript. **Rotate that key** (Browserbase dashboard → Settings → regenerate, then update `.env` and Vaultwarden). The `DEBUG=true` SDK logging is the culprit, not the session-logs API. We don't need `DEBUG=true` again; `bb.sessions.debug()` + `bb.sessions.logs.list()` give us the live-view and CDP logs without dumping the key.

## The timing split (local run, MFA skipped, 3 docs)

```
api-list (sequential):
  GetUserData ................... 1776ms   ← dominates
  GetDocumentsForPolicies primer .. 513ms
  GetPolicySpecificDocsList ....... 204ms
  ───────────────────────────────────────
  api-list TOTAL .............. 2493ms

api-docs (parallel, 3 docs):
  Renters Policy (1.24MB) ...... 1048ms
  New Business App (16KB) ...... 1279ms
  Privacy Statement (9KB) ...... 2842ms   ← slowest despite being smallest
  ───────────────────────────────────────
  api-docs TOTAL .............. 2842ms   (= the slowest doc, they overlap)

FULL fetchDocuments ........... 5335ms
```

So your original guess was about half right. The **byte fetching per doc is ~1-1.3s**, but the parallel batch is gated by whichever doc the server is slowest to render. This run the 9KB Privacy Statement took 2.8s while the 1.24MB Renters doc took 1s. That's pure server-side variance, not our network. Size doesn't predict it.

## The clear lever, now measured

`GetUserData` alone is 1.78s, the single biggest call. It doesn't depend on the primer. The bonus probe ran both in parallel:

```
GetUserData + primer in parallel ... 768ms   (vs 2289ms sequential)
```

That's **~1.5s saved** for free. Parallelizing those two would drop `api-list` from ~2.5s to ~1s, taking full `fetchDocuments` from ~5.3s to roughly **~3.8s**.

## Two caveats on these numbers

1. **This was a local run**, so the `page.request` API calls went out from your Boston machine, not Fly `iad`. On prod (datacenter IP, closer to Allstate's origin) the per-call latency will likely be *similar or a bit faster*, but we still have not confirmed the **source-IP question** with a datacenter IP. It worked from a residential IP here, which is a good sign the endpoints don't IP-gate, but Fly's IP is the real test.

2. **`api-docs` variance is real.** 5.3s on a good run, but if two docs both hit the slow path it could push higher. Worth running it 2-3 more times to see the spread before trusting a single number.

Want me to (a) apply the GetUserData+primer parallelization to the branch and re-measure, (b) deploy the branch to prod and run it there to settle the source-IP + real latency, or both?

assistant
Two parts. Let me answer the parallelization first, then do the branch restructure.

## Simplest way to parallelize

The dependency chain is the key. Three calls:
- `GetUserData` → gives the policy number
- `GetDocumentsForPolicies` (primer) → takes no input, just needs to run before the list
- `GetPolicySpecificDocsList` → needs **both** the policy number AND the primer to have run

So `GetUserData` and the primer don't depend on each other. The simplest fix is one `Promise.all` around those two, then the list call after. No new helpers, no restructure:

```ts
// GetUserData (→ policy number) and the primer don't depend on each other,
// so fire them together; the list call below needs both finished.
const [ud] = (await Promise.all([
  api.get(`${origin}/api/secured/GetUserData`, { headers }).then((r) => r.json()),
  api.get(`${origin}/api/secured/document/GetDocumentsForPolicies`, { headers }),
])) as [{ policies?: { policyImage?: { number?: string } }[] }, unknown];
const policyNumber = ud.policies?.[0]?.policyImage?.number;
```

That's it. `.then(r => r.json())` so the first slot resolves to the parsed body, second slot we ignore. Measured ~1.5s saved.

assistant
Good. Secrets `BROWSERBASE_API_KEY` / `PROJECT_ID` are set; Allstate creds come via the `/login` request body. One machine in `iad`, running the old UI version (deployed 06-19).

Note: after you rotate the leaked Browserbase key, you'll also need `fly secrets set BROWSERBASE_API_KEY=...` since prod uses it too. The current key still works for now.

My approach for safe, useful instrumentation:
- Put debug code behind `ALLSTATE_DEBUG`, off by default.
- **Install the network sniff at the start of `fetchDocuments`, not at login**, so it never sees the password. Doc-phase traffic only.
- Redact bodies (blobs, long digit runs like policy numbers), and never log request headers (no cookie/xsrf leak).
- Log full primer + GetUserData response bodies so we can see if we can **collapse a call** (the real remaining lever, since the call chain is near its parallelization limit).

Write  {"file_path":"/home/dan/Projects/Take_Home/src/debug.ts","content":"// Opt-in carrier instrumentation, enabled with ALLSTATE_DEBUG=1. Off by default.\n// Used to profile the live full-API doc fetch from prod: a redacted network sniff\n// of the document phase plus per-call timing. Secrets are never logged: request\n// headers (cookie / xsrf) are skipped entirely, and bodies are redacted.\nimport type { Page } from \"playwright\";\n\nexport const DEBUG = !!process.env.ALLSTATE_DEBUG;\n\n// Best-effort redaction for anything we print. On prod the Allstate creds arrive\n// in the request body (not env), so the password regex is the backstop; we also\n// collapse long token/base64 blobs and long digit runs (policy numbers).\nconst ENV_SECRETS = [process.env.ALLSTATE_PASSWORD, process.env.ALLSTATE_USER].filter(Boolean) as string[];\nexport function redact(s: string): string {\n  let out = s;\n  for (const sec of ENV_SECRETS) out = out.split(sec).join(\"[REDACTED]\");\n  out = out.replace(/(\"?password\"?\\s*[:=]\\s*\"?)[^\"&,}\\s]+/gi, \"$1[REDACTED]\");\n  out = out.replace(/[A-Za-z0-9+/=_-]{60,}/g, (m) => `<blob ${m.length}b>`);\n  out = out.replace(/\\b\\d{8,}\\b/g, \"<num>\");\n  return out;\n}\n\nexport function dlog(...args: unknown[]): void {\n  if (DEBUG) console.log(\"[allstate-debug]\", ...args);\n}\n\n// Time an async step; transparent (no overhead, no logging) when DEBUG is off.\nexport async function timed<T>(label: string, fn: () => Promise<T>): Promise<T> {\n  if (!DEBUG) return fn();\n  const t = Date.now();\n  try {\n    return await fn();\n  } finally {\n    console.log(`[allstate-debug] STEP ${label.padEnd(34)} ${Date.now() - t}ms`);\n  }\n}\n\n// Redacted request/response logger for allstate.com traffic. Call it at the start\n// of the document phase (after login) so the password never passes through here.\nexport function installNetSniff(page: Page): void {\n  if (!DEBUG) return;\n  const API = /\\/api\\/secured\\//;\n  page.on(\"request\", (req) => {\n    const u = req.url();\n    if (!u.includes(\"allstate.com\")) return;\n    if (API.test(u)) {\n      console.log(`[net] >>> ${req.method()} ${u}`);\n      const pd = req.postData();\n      if (pd) console.log(`[net]     body: ${redact(pd).slice(0, 300)}`);\n    } else {\n      console.log(`[net] >>> ${req.method()} ${new URL(u).pathname}`);\n    }\n  });\n  page.on(\"response\", async (r) => {\n    const u = r.url();\n    if (!u.includes(\"allstate.com\")) return;\n    if (API.test(u)) {\n      const ct = r.headers()[\"content-type\"] || \"\";\n      let body = \"\";\n      if (/json/i.test(ct)) {\n        try {\n          body = redact(await r.text()).slice(0, 500);\n        } catch {}\n      }\n      console.log(`[net] <<< ${r.status()} ${u} ct=${ct.slice(0, 30)} body=${body}`);\n    } else {\n      console.log(`[net] <<< ${r.status()} ${new URL(u).pathname}`);\n    }\n  });\n}\n"}

tool result
#!/bin/sh
# bwget - Bitwarden CLI wrapper that works around the v2025.x/2026.x session regression.
#
# Background: since ~2025.10, `bw unlock` no longer persists the unlocked key to
# data.json, so a BW_SESSION token from one process is ignored by the next. Every
# command treats the vault as locked and reprompts for the master password; in a
# non-TTY that prompt crashes with ERR_USE_AFTER_CLOSE. We sidestep all of it by
# feeding the master password on stdin, which satisfies the reprompt non-interactively.
#
# Usage:  bwget list items | jq ...
#         bwget get password <id>
#         bwget get item <id>
# Password is read from a 0600 file (override with BW_PW_FILE). Never passed on argv/env.

set -eu

PW_FILE="${BW_PW_FILE:-$HOME/.config/bwcli/master.pass}"

if [ ! -r "$PW_FILE" ]; then
    printf 'bwget: cannot read password file: %s\n' "$PW_FILE" >&2
    exit 1
fi

# First line of the file is the master password; pipe it to satisfy the unlock prompt.
head -n 1 "$PW_FILE" | bw "$@"

28	    const fields = requireStrings(req.body, ["carrier"]);

If MFA is required, drop the SMS code into scripts/prod-mfa.txt.\n//   ! node --env-file=.env --import tsx scripts/prod-drive.ts\nimport { existsSync, readFileSync, rmSync, writeFileSync } from \"node:fs\";\n\nconst BASE = process.env.PROD_URL ?? \"https://take-home-policy-puller.fly.dev\";\nconst carrier = \"allstate\";\n\nasync function post(path: string, body: unknown): Promise<{ status: number; json: any }> {\n  const t = Date.now();\n  const r = await fetch(BASE + path, {\n    method: \"POST\",\n    headers: { \"content-type\": \"application/json\" },\n    body: JSON.stringify(body),\n  });\n  const json = await r.json().catch(() => null);\n  console.log(`[client] POST ${path} -> ${r.status} (${Date.now() - t}ms wall)`);\n  return { status: r.status, json };\n}\n\nfunction summarize(documents: any[] | undefined): void {\n  if (!documents) {\n    console.log(\"no documents in response\");\n    return;\n  }\n  for (const d of documents) console.log(`  doc: ${d.name} | ${d.contentType} | base64 len=${(d.bytes || \"\").length}`);\n  writeFileSync(\n    \"/tmp/prod-docs-meta.json\",\n    JSON.stringify(documents.map((d) => ({ name: d.name, contentType: d.contentType, len: (d.bytes || \"\").length })), null, 2),\n  );\n}\n\nconst prep = await post(\"/prepare\", { carrier });\nconst warmId = prep.json?.warmId;\nconsole.log(\"prepare:\", prep.status, warmId ? \"warmId ok\" : JSON.stringify(prep.json));\n\nconst login = await post(\"/login\", {\n  carrier,\n  username: process.env.ALLSTATE_USER,\n  password: [REDACTED],\n  warmId,\n});\nconsole.log(\"login:\", login.status, login.json?.status ?? JSON.stringify(login.json));\n\nlet documents = login.json?.documents;\nif (login.json?.status === \"mfa_needed\") {\n  const sessionId = login.json.sessionId;\n  console.log(\">>> MFA needed. Drop the SMS code into scripts/prod-mfa.txt\");\n  let code = \"\";\n  for (let i = 0; i < 150; i++) {\n    if (existsSync(\"scripts/prod-mfa.txt\")) {\n      code = readFileSync(\"scripts/prod-mfa.txt\", \"utf8\").trim();\n      rmSync(\"scripts/prod-mfa.txt\");\n      break;\n    }\n    await new Promise((r) => setTimeout(r, 2000));\n  }\n  if (!code) {\n    console.log(\"no code, aborting\");\n    process.exit(1);\n  }\n  const mfa = await post(\"/mfa\", { sessionId, code });\n  console.log(\"mfa:\", mfa.status, mfa.json?.status ?? JSON.stringify(mfa.json));\n  documents = mfa.json?.documents;\n}\n\nsummarize(documents);\nconsole.log(\"done.\");\nprocess.exit(0);\n"}

assistant
Here's the Assurant carrier, mapped stage by stage. It implements the same `Carrier` interface as Allstate, but the portal is very different.

## The flow

**prepare()** — open Browserbase session, `goto` the Assurant login, wait for the Okta widget's `#okta-signin-username`. Pre-warmable (no creds needed).

**login()** — fill `#okta-signin-username` / `#okta-signin-password`, click `#okta-signin-submit`. Then a race:
- `input[name="answer"]` appears → MFA needed
- URL goes to `/app/policy/` → dashboard (trusted device, no MFA)
- neither → throw `InvalidCredentialsError` (Okta just re-renders the form with an error, so we treat it as bad creds → 401)

On MFA, it clicks "Send code" and returns `mfaRequired: true`.

**submitMfa()** — fill `answer`, click the Verify/Submit button (matched on `/verify|submit/` because Okta's submit input's accessible name is "Submit button", not "Verify"), wait for `/app/policy/`. Fail → `InvalidMfaError`.

**fetchDocuments()** —
1. If on the `/selection` list, click the policy (`REN\d+` text) → wait for `snapshot`.
2. Click the "view policy documents" card → wait for `/Policy/Documents`.
3. Wait for the "Policy documents" heading.
4. Find every Download button/link. **If zero → `DocumentsUnavailableError` (502).**
5. Click each (sequential clicks, parallel download awaits), collect bytes via the Browserbase Downloads API, `validateDocuments`.

## How it differs from Allstate

| | Allstate (`main`) | Assurant |
|---|---|---|
| auth | custom SPA | **Okta widget** |
| bad-creds handling | `CarrierError` "unexpected page" + screenshot | `InvalidCredentialsError` → 401 |
| doc fetch | **full-API** (`page.request` JSON) | **UI nav + download-to-storage** (the old, slower path) |
| doc names | real titles from the table | **opaque filenames** (GUID, `.pdf` stripped) |
| latency | ~5.3s | ~7.8s |

## Three things worth discussing

1. **Doc naming is weak.** Assurant docs are named by their download filename (an opaque GUID), not a human title like "Confirmation of Coverage." Allstate's full-API gives real titles. This is a visible quality gap in the demo.

2. **No full-API path here.** Assurant still uses the slower UI-download approach (~7.8s, just under budget). We never investigated whether Assurant has a JSON document endpoint like Allstate's. It meets the 8s target, so it was never pushed, but it's the obvious parallel optimization.

3. **The "pending policy" problem.** When you set this up, your policy was Pending (effective 06/20 = today), so only the Confirmation of Coverage was available and the Current Documents table was empty. If the table is still empty on a run, you get the 502. **Today is the effective date**, so the policy may now be active with more docs.

Given Assurant isn't throttled and today is your policy's effective date, the natural move is to **run Assurant against prod now** as the cooldown sanity check, and see whether the Current Documents table populated. Want me to do that, or would you rather dig into one of the three issues first (doc naming, the full-API question, or resilience)?

assistant
Big find. Assurant has the same kind of internal API as Allstate, and your policy **processed today** (the doc-list shows a real document with `BatchName: CAVDD13D_06202026...`), so the Current Documents table is now populated, not just the Confirmation of Coverage.

## The Assurant internal APIs (all on `manage.myassurantpolicy.com`, same-origin, cookie-authed)

1. **Doc list** — `GET /api/Policies/{policyNumber}/Documents?lob=RI` → JSON:
   ```
   {"Documents":[{"Id":"{D4F306EA-...}","BusinessUnitCode":"Renters","ContentSize":..., ...}]}
   ```
   Real metadata per doc, including an `Id` GUID and a title/type. **This fixes the opaque-filename problem** the current carrier has.

2. **Confirmation of Coverage PDF** — `GET /api/v2/Policies/{policyNumber}/POI/ConfirmationOfCoverage?outputMode=file` → served **directly as `application/pdf`**. This is exactly what's behind the Download button (the browser just wrapped it in a `blob:` URL).

3. **Policy lookup** (the "who am I / which policy" call, like Allstate's `GetUserData`) — `GET https://sf-mfh-prod.assurant.com/Servicing/PolicyManagementApi/api/v2/Policies` → JSON array of policies with the policy number.

**Auth:** the `manage.myassurantpolicy.com/api/...` calls ride the ASP.NET session cookie (`ASP.NET_SessionId`, `sid`), so `page.request` in-browser carries them automatically. Same as Allstate. (Same unverified source-IP caveat applies.)

## What this means

We can build an Assurant **full-API** path mirroring Allstate:
- policy number → `GET /api/Policies/{n}/Documents?lob=RI` (list) → fetch each PDF directly.
- Faster than the ~7.8s UI-download path, and with real document names.

## One gap

The probe only captured the **Confirmation of Coverage** PDF endpoint. The newly-arrived **Current Documents** table entries (the ones with `Id` GUIDs) are fetched by a different endpoint we didn't trigger, probably `/api/Policies/{n}/Documents/{Id}` or similar. One more focused probe (click an actual table-row download) would capture it.

So: want me to (a) run a second probe to nail the by-`Id` document-fetch endpoint, then (b) build the Assurant full-API carrier? Or just build it now with the Confirmation-of-Coverage endpoint and add the table docs after?

assistant
Nailed it. The click-capture gave ground truth, and it's **not** a by-`Id` endpoint (my `Documents/{id}` guesses 500'd, as you can see). Here's the real picture.

## The per-document endpoint (Current Documents table)

The table row's download anchor is:
```
GET /api/Policies/{policyNumber}/Document?documentToken={TOKEN}&lob=RI   → application/pdf
```
- `Document` (singular), authed by a **per-document `documentToken`** (an opaque base64 blob), not the `Id` GUID.
- The token lives in the **rendered anchor href** (`ng-click="vm.trackEventDocumentView(...)"`), and is **not** a field in the `/Documents` JSON (the JSON has ~70 fields, none a token). So the token comes from the page render, not the list call.

## The Confirmation of Coverage endpoint (always-available)
```
GET /api/v2/Policies/{policyNumber}/POI/ConfirmationOfCoverage?outputMode=file   → application/pdf
```
Pure API, no token. This is the one behind the "Download" button.

## The doc-list JSON gives real names
`GET /api/Policies/{n}/Documents?lob=RI` → each doc has `DocumentTitle` ("REN-6650172-00") and `DocumentType` ("Declaration Page/New/Renewal..."), plus `ContentSize`, `DateCreated`, `MimeType`. **This fixes the opaque-filename problem.**

## What this means for the carrier

Two PDF sources, both `application/pdf` via `page.request` (cookie-authed, same-origin):
- **POI**: pure API, no token needed.
- **Table docs**: need the per-doc `documentToken`, which only appears in the rendered documents page.

So the clean design is a **hybrid**: render `/Policy/Documents` (we already do), scrape the `a[href*=documentToken]` hrefs + the POI endpoint, then `page.request.get` all of them in parallel for the bytes. That drops the popup → Browserbase-storage → Downloads-API polling we do today (faster), and names docs from `DocumentType`/anchor text instead of GUID filenames.

A *pure* no-render API path isn't available unless we find where that `documentToken` is minted (it's server-encrypted, not in the list JSON).

Want me to build the hybrid Assurant fetch (scrape hrefs + `page.request` the PDFs in parallel, real names), or do one more probe to chase the token's origin for a pure-API path first?

assistant
Yes, found it, and it's much simpler than it looked. The token isn't encrypted-and-hidden at all. Line 179 of `documents-list.component.js`:

```js
return "" + App.serverEndpoint + apiUrl + "?documentToken=" + encodeURIComponent(document.DocId) + "&lob=" + this.parent.policyDetails.LOB;
```

**`documentToken = encodeURIComponent(DocId)`.** The token is literally the `DocId` field from the documents-list JSON. `DocId`'s *value* happens to be a base64 blob (that's why it looked like an opaque encrypted token), but it's sitting right there in the `/Documents` response, one of the ~70 fields.

That also explains my failed by-Id test earlier: I used the wrong field (`Id`, the `{D4F306EA...}` GUID) and the wrong path (`Documents/{id}`). The real one is `Document?documentToken={DocId}`.

## So pure-API IS possible for Assurant, no render needed

1. **List** — `GET /api/Policies/{n}/Documents?lob=RI` → each doc has `DocId` (the token) + `DocumentType`/`DocumentTitle` (real names).
2. **Each doc** — `GET /api/Policies/{n}/Document?documentToken={encodeURIComponent(DocId)}&lob=RI` → `application/pdf`.
3. **Confirmation of Coverage** — `GET /api/v2/Policies/{n}/POI/ConfirmationOfCoverage?outputMode=file` → `application/pdf` (no token).

`App.serverEndpoint` is empty (same origin), `apiUrl` = `/api/Policies/{policyId}/Document`, `LOB` = `RI` for renters. All cookie-authed, so `page.request` carries it, exactly like Allstate. (Same ID-card pattern at line 152, for free.)

So Assurant can go full pure-API just like Allstate, faster than today's download path and with real document names. We found it in source; I'd confirm it live when building (one more login/MFA).

Want me to build the pure-API Assurant carrier now and verify it live?

=== types.ts ===
// ---- the wire contract (shared by backend and, later, the frontend) ----

// A policy document. Usually a PDF, but not guaranteed, so we carry the real
// contentType (from the download's Content-Type header) and never hardcode it.
export type Document = {
    name: string;
    contentType: string; // MIME, e.g. "application/pdf"
    bytes: string; // base64
};

// what /login returns
export type LoginResponse =
    | { status: "mfa_needed"; sessionId: string }
    | { status: "done"; sessionId: string; documents: Document[] };

// what /mfa returns
export type MfaResponse = { status: "done"; documents: Document[] };

// what any endpoint returns on failure, with a non-2xx status. Failures are
// status codes + this body, not a "failed" field on the success types.
export type ErrorResponse = { error: string };

// ---- internal session state machine ----

export type SessionState =
    | "WARMING"
    | "WARM"
    | "LOGGING_IN"
    | "AWAITING_MFA"
    | "SUBMITTING_MFA"
    | "FETCHING_DOCS"
    | "DONE"
    | "FAILED";

// ---- the plug every carrier implements ----

export interface Carrier {
    readonly name: string;
    // open the browser and load the login form, no credentials yet. Optional to
    // call ahead of login() to pre-warm while the user is still typing.
    prepare(): Promise<void>;
    // start the login. returns whether the portal then asks for an MFA code.
    login(username: string, password: [REDACTED] Promise<{ mfaRequired: boolean }>;
    // type the MFA code the user received.
    submitMfa(code: string): Promise<void>;
    // pull the policy documents once logged in.
    fetchDocuments(): Promise<Document[]>;
    // tear down the browser session.
    close(): Promise<void>;
}
=== documents.ts ===
import type { Document } from "./types.js";
import { DocumentsUnavailableError } from "./errors.js";

export const EXPECTED_CONTENT_TYPE = "application/pdf";

export type DocumentWarning = { name: string; reason: string };

// Returns a warning if the doc looks off, else null. Never throws: a malformed
// but present document beats failing the whole fetch.
export function inspectDocument(doc: Document): DocumentWarning | null {
    if (!doc.bytes) {
        return { name: doc.name, reason: "empty body" };
    }
    if (!doc.contentType.startsWith(EXPECTED_CONTENT_TYPE)) {
        return { name: doc.name, reason: `unexpected contentType "${doc.contentType}"` };
    }
    return null;
}

// Throws if the scrape produced nothing: these carriers always have >=1 document,
// so an empty result means the fetch broke, not that the user has none. A silent
// empty "success" is the worst failure for a tool whose job is pulling documents.
// Otherwise logs suspicious-but-present docs and returns them.
export function validateDocuments(carrier: string, docs: Document[]): Document[] {
    if (docs.length === 0) {
        throw new DocumentsUnavailableError(`${carrier}: no documents were retrieved`);
    }
    for (const doc of docs) {
        const warning = inspectDocument(doc);
        if (warning) console.warn(`[${carrier}] "${warning.name}": ${warning.reason}`);
    }
    return docs;
}

assistant
The verify failed at the doc-list call: it returned **"Unauthorized"** (not JSON). So `page.request` with cookies alone isn't enough, the Assurant API needs something more (almost certainly an Okta `Authorization: [REDACTED] header that the Angular app adds, which our raw request doesn't). Login + MFA themselves worked fine.

Good news: the byte-fetch design is right, we just need the auth header. Let me run one combined probe to pin down exactly what header the app sends and confirm a replay works with it (so we only spend one more MFA).

Crucial finding before the crash: the Assurant API auth is a **`Authorization: [REDACTED] <JWT>` (Okta token, len 1276)** plus a `ui-transaction` header. That's what our `page.request` was missing → "Unauthorized." The probe then crashed on a tsx/`page.evaluate` quirk (`__name is not defined`), but it already gave us the answer.

That same `__name` bug would hit the fingerprint probe, so let me fix its `evaluate` calls to string form first, then run it and grab the Fly egress IP.

assistant
Bot-test screenshot is mostly green (WebDriver present: passed, Chrome: passed, real WebGL, etc.). So the **browser itself is well-disguised**. Here's the full picture and the brainstorm.

## What we're up against (from the probe)

| Surface | Browser (login, page loads) | `page.request` (our API calls) |
|---|---|---|
| **Egress IP** | residential — Charter/Spectrum, Texas (Browserbase proxy) | **datacenter** — Latitude.sh/Fly, Virginia (on prod) |
| **TLS/JA3** | real Chrome 149 (`ja3 325bfb…`, `ja4 t13d…h2`) | **Node/undici** (`ja3 d3d0a6…`, `ja4 t13d5212`) — not Chrome |
| **UA** | Chrome 149 | Chrome 149 (but TLS says Node → mismatch) |
| webdriver | false | n/a |

WAFs: **Allstate = Akamai Bot Manager** (`_abck`, `bm_sz`, `ak_bmsc`), **Assurant = Cloudflare Bot Management** (`cf-ray`, `__cf_bm`).

**The key insight:** the IP is only half of it. Your `page.request` calls have *two* bot tells, a datacenter IP **and** a Node TLS fingerprint that doesn't match the Chrome UA they claim. The browser is clean; the Node-side API calls are the exposed part.

## How to fix it, options

**Option A — run the fetch inside the browser (recommended).** Replace `page.request.get(...)` with `page.evaluate(() => fetch(...))`. The fetch then executes in the page's JS context, so it inherits the browser's **residential IP, real Chrome TLS, and session cookies automatically**. Our API calls become indistinguishable from the app's own XHR. Fixes the IP *and* the TLS tell in one move. Cost: binary PDFs need a base64 conversion in-page, and we pass our computed headers (Allstate XSRF / Assurant Bearer) explicitly. Applies to both carriers.

**Option B — route `page.request` through a residential proxy.** A separate `APIRequestContext` with a proxy. Needs proxy creds, and it still doesn't fix the Node TLS fingerprint. Worse than A.

**Option C — revert to UI download** (clicks → browser download). In-browser/residential, but slow (~12s Allstate) and back to opaque filenames. We'd be undoing the work.

**Option D — accept it for Allstate only.** Allstate's API returned 200 with `_abck=-1`, so it may not gate on IP/TLS for `/api/secured`. Risky bet given the TLS tell, and doesn't help Assurant behind Cloudflare.

## My recommendation

**Option A for both carriers.** It's the smallest change that makes the API calls ride the same residential IP + Chrome fingerprint as the login, so the whole flow looks like one real browser session. It also removes the unverified source-IP caveat entirely (no more "does the datacenter IP work" question, because we stop using it).

Since A changes how we fetch anyway, I'd hold the Assurant live re-verify until after I convert it (so we spend one MFA, not two).

Want me to convert both carriers' API fetches to in-browser `fetch` (Option A) and then verify Assurant live?


user

Use this for carriers' internal-API document calls. Returns the body\n    // as base64 (works for JSON and binary alike). The page function is passed as\n    // a string with args inlined so tsx/esbuild can't inject its `__name` helper\n    // (undefined in the page context) into the evaluated function.\n    async fetchInPage(\n        url: string,\n        opts: { method?: string; headers?: Record<string, string>; body?: string } = {},\n    ): Promise<{ status: number; contentType: string; base64: string }> {\n        const init = {\n            method: opts.method ?? \"GET\",\n            headers: opts.headers ?? {},\n            body: opts.body, // JSON.stringify drops this key when undefined (GET)\n            credentials: \"include\",\n        };\n        const expr = `(async () => {\n            const r = await fetch(${JSON.stringify(url)}, ${JSON.stringify(init)});\n            const bytes = new Uint8Array(await r.arrayBuffer());\n            let binary = \"\";\n            for (let i = 0; i < bytes.length; i += 0x8000)\n                binary += String.fromCharCode.apply(null, bytes.subarray(i, i + 0x8000));\n            return { status: r.status, contentType: r.headers.get(\"content-type\") || \"\", base64: btoa(binary) };\n        })()`;\n        return this.page.evaluate(expr) as Promise<{ status: number; contentType: string; base64: string }>;\n    }\n\n    // Given items a carrier already triggered (each a display name + the filename\n    // the download produced), wait for the files to sync, pair them by filename,"}

Option A is **confirmed working**, proven against our own echo server without touching a single carrier:

```
IN-PAGE fetch   → server saw x-forwarded-for: 76.129.35.39, 66.241.125.47
page.request    → server saw x-forwarded-for: 71.233.64.118, 66.241.125.47
                                              └─ our Node process (Fly datacenter IP on prod)
                              └─ residential proxy IP  ✓
```

The first hop in each `x-forwarded-for` is the real origin (the second, `66.241.125.47`, is Fly's edge appending itself):
- **In-page `fetch`** egressed from `76.129.35.39` — the **residential proxy** (a different residential IP than the earlier probe, because each session gets a fresh one). 
- **`page.request`** egressed from `71.233.64.118` — our Node process (my Comcast IP locally; the Fly datacenter IP on prod).

So switching the carrier API calls to in-page `fetch` makes them ride the residential IP + Chrome TLS, exactly like the app's own XHR. The datacenter-IP and Node-TLS tells are both gone.

I have everything I need from the static review. Here is my adversarial assessment.

# Adversarial Assessment: Browser-Automation Carrier Document Puller

## Executive framing

The `fetchInPage` switch was the right instinct and closes the single most obvious tell (the Node/undici TLS + datacenter IP split). But it is not a complete fix. An in-page `fetch()` is still observably different from a real Angular XHR, and several behavioral, telemetry, and OPSEC tells remain. The most dangerous ones are not the TLS fingerprint you already fixed; they are (1) the missing telemetry/correlation headers that the carriers' own bot products expect to see attached to the document calls, (2) the unhuman timing/burst pattern, and (3) the residential-then-datacenter split that still exists on the Browserbase control plane and the Allstate `page.request` fallback note. Your own probe already gives you the smoking gun on the last point: `_abck` stays `-1` (unvalidated). That is not "we're safe," that is "Akamai has not yet been asked to make a decision on this session." When Allstate turns up enforcement, an unvalidated `_abck` is an instant block.

---

## Findings, ranked by likelihood of being flagged

### CRITICAL-1 — `_abck = -1` is a latent kill switch, not a green light
`latency.md` line 67-69 and the CLAUDE.md gotcha both treat `_abck=-1` as "it is NOT anti-bot." That conclusion is wrong and is the single biggest risk in the whole design.

`_abck` ending in `~-1~` means the Akamai sensor has not posted a valid sensor payload, so the session is **unvalidated**. Many Akamai deployments run in "monitor"/log-only mode on internal API paths and only enforce on login or on specific endpoints. You are currently passing because the policy `/api/secured/*` endpoints are not in enforcing mode for that cookie state. The moment Allstate flips those endpoints to enforce (a config change on their side, no code deploy), every request with `_abck=-1` gets a 403/abck challenge interstitial and your full-API path dies instantly with no warning. This is fragile by construction: you are depending on the carrier *not* enforcing a control they have already deployed.

Worse: your real Chrome session never POSTs the Akamai sensor data because you drive the UI fast and headlessly and the `_abck` validation handshake (the `bm-sz`/sensor_data POST to the Akamai endpoint) may never complete or may complete with a low trust score. A real browser session that a human used would have a *validated* `_abck`. So you are not just neutral, you are anomalous: a logged-in session that browsed the dashboard but never produced a valid sensor payload is itself a bot signal Akamai can score on later.

**Likelihood of flag: high over any time horizon beyond the demo. Low for a one-shot demo today.**

### CRITICAL-2 — In-page `fetch()` is missing the headers a real Angular XHR sends
This is the core of your question 2. An in-page `fetch()` via `page.evaluate` does **not** automatically reproduce what the SPA's `HttpClient`/XHR sends. Specifically:

- **`sec-fetch-*` headers.** A real `fetch()` from page context *will* get `Sec-Fetch-Site`, `Sec-Fetch-Mode`, `Sec-Fetch-Dest` injected by Chrome (these are forbidden headers, browser-controlled), so those are actually correct for you. Good. But `Sec-Fetch-Site` will be `same-origin` only if the URL origin matches the page origin. You build `origin` from `LOGIN_URL` (`myaccountrwd.allstate.com`) and the API is on the same host, so that is fine for Allstate. For Assurant, confirm `manage.myassurantpolicy.com` is the page origin when you fire the API calls; if the SPA navigated to a different subdomain, `Sec-Fetch-Site` flips to `same-site`/`cross-site` and diverges from the app's own calls.
- **`x-requested-with`.** Many Angular apps (and Allstate's specifically, given the `app-name`/`x-efm` convention) attach app-specific correlation headers via an HTTP interceptor on *every* call. You hardcode `app-name: MYA` and `x-efm: true` for Al
… [truncated 17633 chars]


assistant
Pentester came back with a sharp, blunt assessment. The headline: `fetchInPage` was the right move and kills the TLS/datacenter tell, but it's **not** a complete cloak, and our docs are over-claiming safety. Distilled:

## The critical ones

**1. `_abck=-1` is a latent kill switch, not "not anti-bot."** Our framing in `latency.md`/`CLAUDE.md` ("it is NOT anti-bot, Akamai `_abck=-1` but 200s") is wrong and dangerous. `-1` means Akamai hasn't *validated* the session, not that it's absent. Those `/api/secured` endpoints are in monitor/log-only mode right now; the day Allstate flips them to enforce (a config change, no deploy), every `_abck=-1` request 403s and the full-API path dies with no warning. Worse: a logged-in session that never produced a valid Akamai sensor payload is itself a scorable anomaly.

**2. The in-page fetch still diverges from a real Angular XHR.**
- Our hardcoded `referer` in `allstate.ts` is **silently ignored**, `Referer` is a forbidden header `fetch()` won't let JS set. Chrome sets the real one from the current page, which is the *dashboard*, not the documents page. So our request ordering says "fired doc calls without ever being on the docs page", an ordering anomaly a human never produces.
- We **curate** 4 headers (`app-name`, `x-efm`, xsrf) instead of replaying the full set the SPA sends (`x-sid/x-vid/x-tid` correlation IDs, etc.). Minimal-but-clean header sets are themselves distinctive.

**3. The parallel burst is the loudest behavioral tell.** Our `Promise.all` fan-out of all `GetUdpRetrieveDocument` / Assurant PDF calls fires N identical authed POSTs within milliseconds, zero mouse/scroll/dwell between them. That's a textbook bot signature, especially for Cloudflare (Assurant), which scores cadence hard. Ironic, since we *reverted* parallel UI clicks earlier; the API path has no natural throttle so we ended up more aggressive than the real app.

## Other notable
- **`hardwareConcurrency=2` on a residential IP is contradictory** (residential ISP + 2-core = cloud browser). A scorable mismatch in Akamai's sensor.
- **Okta velocity risk:** same account, rotating residential IPs, many short sessions = looks like credential-stuffing; can trigger step-up MFA or lock you out mid-demo.
- **Bearer [REDACTED] is fragile:** no audience check (grabs whatever Bearer [REDACTED] last), and we reuse one static `ui-transaction` across all N calls (the real app generates a fresh one per call).
- **Hygiene:** remove `/debug/echo` before submit; post-login failure screenshots leak PII (policy #, address).

## The real tension for this take-home

The take-home wants **both** sub-8s latency **and** anti-bot handling. Those pull opposite directions here: the parallel burst is fast but loud; the pentester's fix (hybrid: navigate to the docs page → serialized, jittered fetches → full replayed headers) is stealthy but adds seconds. His bottom line matches that nuance: **for a one-shot demo against your own accounts today, ship the full-API path, it works.** But the *production-ready* characterization should be honest, and the most defensible posture is the hybrid.

## What I'd actually do (cheap, high-value)
1. **Fix the docs framing** (`latency.md`/`CLAUDE.md`): stop saying "not anti-bot"; state it works because endpoints aren't presently enforcing + the residential/validated-cookie session. Honest characterization is itself a point in the FDE review.
2. **Remove `/debug/echo` + `DEBUG_ECHO`/`ALLSTATE_DEBUG`** before submit; scrub/scope the screenshots.
3. **Drop the dead hardcoded `referer`** (it's ignored anyway).

Bigger, optional (more time): the hybrid path + serialized/jittered fetches + full header replay + `hardwareConcurrency` fix.

Which do you want to act on? I'd at least do 1-3 now (cheap, and #1 is a credibility win), and we can decide on the hybrid given the latency tradeoff.

user
ok so then what are the biggest "bot" tells that were currently running?


assistant
What we've already neutralized (Option A): the datacenter IP and the Node/undici TLS mismatch. Those are gone, the doc calls now ride the residential IP + real Chrome TLS. What's left, biggest first:

**1. `_abck=-1` unvalidated Akamai session (Allstate).** Our session never produces an accepted Akamai sensor payload, so the cookie stays unvalidated. It works only because those endpoints aren't enforcing right now. This is the single biggest one, a config flip on Allstate's side kills it instantly, and an authed session with an unvalidated `_abck` is itself scorable.

**2. The parallel burst (both carriers, loudest for Assurant/Cloudflare).** We fire all the doc fetches at once via `Promise.all`, N identical authed requests within milliseconds, zero mouse/scroll/dwell between them. No human and not even the real SPA does that. Cloudflare scores cadence hard. Ironically it's a more aggressive pattern than the UI clicks we deliberately throttled earlier.

**3. Request ordering / wrong page context (Allstate).** We fire the doc API calls while the browser is still on the dashboard, never navigating to the documents page. So `Referer`/`Sec-Fetch` and the request sequence say "pulled documents without ever visiting the documents page", an ordering a real user can't produce. (Our hardcoded `referer` doesn't even help, `fetch` ignores it.)

**4. `hardwareConcurrency=2` on a residential IP.** Residential ISP + 2-core machine = cloud browser. A direct contradiction Akamai's sensor can read.

**5. Curated header set + static `ui-transaction` (Assurant).** We send a minimal 4-header subset; the real app attaches correlation IDs we omit, and we reuse one `ui-transaction` across all N calls while the app mints a fresh one per call. A clean, distinctive footprint.

Underneath all of it: **account velocity**, same policy account hit from many rotating residential IPs in short identical sessions, which to Okta looks like credential-stuffing and can trigger step-up MFA or a lockout (this is partly what bit us on Allstate earlier today).

The throughline: our **network fingerprint is now clean, but our behavior isn't.** The remaining tells are all about *how* we act (cadence, ordering, request graph) and the *cloud-vs-residential* contradictions, not the IP/TLS anymore. The hybrid posture (UI nav to the docs page → serialized, jittered, full-header fetches) is what closes #2, #3, and #5 together, at a few seconds' latency cost.

_abck=-1 unvalidated Akamai session so is there any known fix to this?


assistant
Short answer: [REDACTED] in principle, but it's the part Akamai is specifically built to make hard, so there's no clean flag, you have to actually pass the sensor.

## What `_abck=-1` actually means

Allstate loads Akamai's sensor JS (those `/cwnbKR/...` and `/akam/...` requests we saw in the sniff). That script collects device + behavioral telemetry and POSTs an encrypted `sensor_data` blob back to Akamai. When Akamai accepts a payload and scores it human-likely, `_abck` flips from the trailing `~-1~` (unvalidated) to a validated state. `-1` = "no accepted sensor payload yet." So "fixing" it = getting Akamai to accept a sensor submission from our session.

## The legitimate fix (what a real browser does)

1. **Don't block the sensor, and let it submit.** We don't block it (good). But our flow logs in and immediately hits the API, the sensor POST may not have completed. Add a step after login: stay on the page, then **wait for the sensor POST** (watch for the `/cwn

tool result
Static review done. Here's the blunt, ranked breakdown. Both files read in full; everything below is grounded in what the code actually does.

## Critical framing

Your single biggest tell is structural, not header-level: you fire `Documents` list + N `Document` PDFs + `ConfirmationOfCoverage` as a `Promise.all` burst from a snapshot page that, in a real session, would NOT be calling those endpoints at all unless the user clicked into a documents view. The real Angular app loads the doc list when the user navigates to a documents tab, then fetches a PDF only when the user clicks a row. You request every PDF the instant the list returns. The `jitter(700)` smear does almost nothing against this: a human doesn't open 4 PDFs within 700ms of each other. Cloudflare's behavioral scorer and Okta's risk engine both see "authenticated principal that enumerates every document with zero think-time." That is the profile to fix, and the cheap version of fixing it is sequential-with-think-time, addressed in item 0 below.

---

## Ranked changes (profile reduction ÷ latency cost)

### 0. Serialize the PDF fetches with think-time instead of a 700ms-jittered parallel burst — HIGHEST value
- **Tell:** N authenticated PDF GETs landing in the same ~700ms window, immediately after the list call, all from a snapshot page. No human reads a doc list and opens 4 PDFs in under a second. Parallel-with-jitter is still parallel; the arrival histogram is a spike.
- **Fix:** Drop `Promise.all`. Fetch the list, then `for` loop the PDFs with `await jitter(...)` between each, sequential. You already have `jitter`. This is a 5-line change in `fetchDocuments`.
- **Latency cost:** This is the one real tradeoff. Sequential N PDFs costs roughly (N-1) x (per-fetch RTT + jitter). With 2-4 docs at a few hundred ms each plus modest jitter, you're looking at ~1-2s added. Given your 7.8s measured vs 8s budget, you likely cannot afford full human pacing. **Compromise: serialize but with small inter-doc gaps (jitter ~250-400ms), not 700.** That alone removes the same-millisecond-burst signature for a few hundred ms total, and is strictly better than parallel+jitter at the same total latency. Net: shift from "parallel, gated by slowest + 700" to "sequential, small gaps" only if the doc count is low (2-4). If N is large, keep parallel but cap concurrency at 2.
- **Breakage risk:** None. Same calls, same headers, just ordered.
- **Verdict:** Best profile-per-ms available to you. Do the bounded-concurrency-2 + small jitter version to stay in budget.

### 1. Mint a fresh `ui-transaction` per call — high value, near-zero latency
- **Tell:** You capture ONE `ui-transaction` from the first Bearer [REDACTED] and replay the identical value on the list call and every PDF call. If `ui-transaction` is a per-request correlation/transaction ID (the name strongly implies exactly that, almost certainly a UUID v4 the Angular app generates per HttpClient call via an interceptor), then N API calls sharing one transaction ID is a dead giveaway server-side. Real clients emit a unique one per request. A static repeated value is a textbook replay signature and is trivially detectable in Assurant's own logs even if Cloudflare misses it.
- **Fix:** Generate a fresh UUID v4 per call. In the page context you already use for `fetchInPage`, `crypto.randomUUID()` is available; or generate in Node with `crypto.randomUUID()` and inject into headers per call. Set `headers["ui-transaction"] = crypto.randomUUID()` inside `fetchPdf` and before the list call, instead of spreading the captured one.
- **Format confidence:** Capture-and-inspect would confirm, but the name + Angular-interceptor convention makes UUID v4 the overwhelming likelihood. **Do not blindly switch without one observed sample** — log the captured value's shape once (length, hyphen positions) in a debug run and match the format. If it's a UUID, mint UUIDs. If it carries structure (prefix, timestamp), mimic that structure.
- **Latency cost:** None
… [truncated 9219 chars]


assistant
Pentester came back with a clear low-latency plan for Assurant. One important correction first: it guessed `ui-transaction` is a per-request UUID, but our earlier auth-probe already captured the real value, it's a **JSON identity blob** (`{"ClientId":"AICBLZ","ClientIdentity":"A…"}`), not a UUID. So reusing the captured value is actually correct (minting a fake UUID would be wrong/could break it). That removes its #1/#2 concern.

The genuinely cheap, high-value wins (ranked):

**1. Replay the full captured header set (zero latency, biggest lever).** Right now we capture only `authorization` + `ui-transaction` and hand-write `accept`. Instead: filter the capture listener to `/api/Policies` requests, grab **all** the app's headers from one, and replay that whole set (minus browser-controlled ones). Our requests become header-identical to the SPA's own XHR instead of a curated 3-header subset. Zero latency, lower breakage risk than now.

**2. Fix `hardwareConcurrency=2` (zero latency).** Set a coherent fingerprint (8 cores + matching deviceMemory) in the Browserbase session config at creation. Kills the "residential IP but 2-core machine" contradiction. Need to confirm Browserbase's fingerprint API key.

**3. Sequential doc fetches (near-zero for Assurant).** The big burst tell. For Assurant this is cheap because you only have ~2 docs (Declarations + Confirmation of Coverage), so fetching them one-after-another with a small gap instead of `Promise.all` adds maybe ~300ms, not seconds. Worth it. (For Allstate with more docs we'd cap concurrency at 2 instead.)

**4. Sticky residential IP for the session (zero latency).** Make sure the Browserbase proxy doesn't rotate the egress IP mid-session, an IP change between login and the fetches is an Okta step-up trigger. Just a config check on `proxies`.

The one thing it flagged as the *biggest* structural tell, that we fetch every doc the instant the list returns from the snapshot page, without ever visiting the documents view, is only fully fixable by navigating to `/Policy/Documents` first (the hybrid), which does add latency. The cheap partial fix is #3 (sequential).

Net: #1, #2, #4 are zero-latency; #3 is ~300ms for Assurant. All four keep you well under 8s.

Want me to implement #1-#4? #1 (full header replay) and #3 (sequential) are quick edits to `assurant.ts`; #2/#4 are Browserbase config in `browserbase.ts` (I'll verify the fingerprint/sticky API against the docs first).

### client.sessions.create

Source: https://github.com/browserbase/sdk-node/blob/main/api.md

Creates a new session. Sessions are used to manage browser instances for tasks like browsing or automation.

```APIDOC
## POST /v1/sessions

### Description
Creates a new session. Sessions are used to manage browser instances for tasks like browsing or automation.

### Method
POST

### Endpoint
/v1/sessions

### Parameters
#### Request Body
- **params** (object) - Required - Parameters for creating the session.

### Response
#### Success Response (200)
- **SessionCreateResponse** (object) - Details of the created session.
```

--------------------------------

### Initialize Browserbase Client

Source: https://github.com/browserbase/sdk-node/blob/main/README.md

Initialize the Browserbase client with your API key. The API key is automatically read from the BROWSERBASE_API_KEY environment variable if not explicitly provided.

```js
import Browserbase from '@browserbasehq/sdk';

const client = new Browserbase({
  apiKey: process.env['BROWSERBASE_API_KEY'], // This is the default and can be omitted
});

const session = await client.sessions.create({ projectId: 'your_project_id' });
```

--------------------------------

### Use TypeScript Types for Session Creation

Source: https://github.com/browserbase/sdk-node/blob/main/README.md

Utilize TypeScript definitions for request parameters and response fields when creating a session. This ensures type safety and provides autocompletion.

```ts
import Browserbase from '@browserbasehq/sdk';

const client = new Browserbase({
  apiKey: process.env['BROWSERBASE_API_KEY'], // This is the default and can be omitted
});

const params: Browserbase.SessionCreateParams = { projectId: 'your_project_id' };
const session: Browserbase.SessionCreateResponse = await client.sessions.create(params);
```

--------------------------------

### Configure Automatic Retries for API Requests

Source: https://github.com/browserbase/sdk-node/blob/main/README.md

Configure the number of automatic retries for API requests. By default, certain errors are retried twice with exponential backoff. This can be disabled or configured globally or per-request.

```js
// Configure the default for all requests:
const client = new Browserbase({
  maxRetries: 0, // default is 2
});

// Or, configure per-request:
await client.sessions.create({ projectId: 'your_project_id' }, {
  maxRetries: 5,
});
```

--------------------------------

### Configure Request Timeouts

Source: https://github.com/browserbase/sdk-node/blob/main/README.md

Set default and per-request timeouts for API calls. The default timeout is 1 minute.

```typescript
const client = new Browserbase({
  timeout: 20 * 1000, // 20 seconds (default is 1 minute)
});

await client.sessions.create({ projectId: 'your_project_id' }, {
  timeout: 5 * 1000,
});
```

### Create Session with US Geolocation (Python)

Source: https://docs.browserbase.com/platform/identity/proxies

This Python code creates a Browserbase session, specifying a proxy's geolocation to a US city, state, and country. The Browserbase library must be installed and initialized with your API key.

```python
from browserbase import Browserbase
import os

bb = Browserbase(api_key=os.environ["BROWSERBASE_API_KEY"])

def create_session_with_geo_location():
  session = bb.sessions.create(
    proxies=[
        {
        "type": "browserbase",
        "geolocation": {
            "city": "NEW_YORK",
            "state": "NY",
            "country": "US"
            }
        }
    ]
  )
  print("Session URL: https://browserbase.com/sessions/" + session.id)
  return session

session = create_session_with_geo_location()

```

--------------------------------

### Create Session with Built-in Proxies (Python)

Source: https://docs.browserbase.com/platform/identity/proxies

Use this snippet to create a new browser session routed through Browserbase's managed residential proxies. Set `proxies` to `True` for this functionality.

```python
from browserbase import Browserbase
import os

def create_session_with_proxies():
  bb = Browserbase(api_key=os.environ["BROWSERBASE_API_KEY"])
  session = bb.sessions.create(
    proxies=True
  )
  print("Session URL: https://browserbase.com/sessions/" + session.id)
  return session

session = create_session_with_proxies()

```

### Create Session with US Geolocation (Node.js)

Source: https://docs.browserbase.com/platform/identity/proxies

Use this snippet to create a new Browserbase session with a proxy set to a specific city, state, and country within the United States. Ensure the Browserbase SDK is imported and initialized with your API key.

```typescript
import { Browserbase } from "@browserbasehq/sdk";

const bb = new Browserbase({ apiKey: process.env.BROWSERBASE_API_KEY! });

async function createSessionWithGeoLocation() {
  const session = await bb.sessions.create({
  proxies: [
    {
      "type": "browserbase",
      "geolocation": {
        "city": "NEW_YORK",
        "state": "NY",
        "country": "US"
      }
    }
  ],
  });
return session;
}
const session = await createSessionWithGeoLocation();
console.log("Session URL: https://browserbase.com/sessions/" + session.id);

### Create an authenticated session with context and proxies

Source: https://docs.browserbase.com/platform/identity/authentication

Use a persistent context ID to reuse authentication state across sessions while routing traffic through specific proxy locations.

```typescript
import { Browserbase } from "@browserbasehq/sdk";

async function createAuthSession(contextId: string) {
  const bb = new Browserbase({ apiKey: process.env.BROWSERBASE_API_KEY! });

  const session = await bb.sessions.create({
    browserSettings: {
      context: {
        id: contextId,
        persist: true
      }
    },
    proxies: [{
      type: "browserbase",
      geolocation: {
        city: "New York",
        state: "NY",
        country: "US"
      }
    }]
  });

  console.log("Session URL: https://browserbase.com/sessions/" + session.id);
  return session;

The run died on the mistyped code (`[MFA]`), Okta 403'd it and the script timed out waiting for the dashboard. Important: that 403 was **our wrong code, not a bot block**, the `authn` calls before it returned 200, so login mechanics are fine. And it captured the whole login-phase fingerprinting before failing, which is exactly what you asked for. Here's the clear picture.

## WHAT WE SHOW (client-readable surface, hardened config)
```
Windows 10 / Chrome 149 / Win32 / vendor Google Inc.
webdriver: false   hardwareConcurrency: 8   deviceMemory: 8   plugins: 5
GPU: NVIDIA GeForce RTX 3060 Ti (Direct3D11)
timezone: America/New_York   locale: en-US        (matches MA proxy ✓)
canvas: stable hash
screen: 1280×720, dpr 1   ← THE NEW TELL
```
Everything is coherent now **except screen resolution**: a 1280×720 / dpr-1 display on a machine with an RTX 3060 Ti is implausible (that's a default cloud viewport; real gaming rigs are 1920×1080+). A fingerprinter correlating GPU↔resolution would catch it. Cheap zero-latency fix: set a realistic viewport (1920×1080) in the Browserbase session config.

## WHERE THEY ATTACK (network)
- **Cloudflare = passive scoring, and we passed.** It planted `__cf_bm` (its bot-management token) on `.myassurantpolicy.com` via Set-Cookie, but served **no challenge / no Turnstile / no `/cdn-cgi/challenge` beacon**. That means CF scored our request silently and let it through. It scores on TLS/JA3, IP reputation, HTTP/2 fingerprint, cadence, all now clean (real Windows Chrome TLS, MA Comcast residential, jittered). (The other `__cf_bm`/`_cfuvid` cookies are from an embedded Vimeo video, noise.)
- **Okta = credentials + SMS + server-side risk, light client fingerprint.** `POST /api/v1/authn` carried just `{username, password, options}` (147 bytes), **no device-fingerprint blob, no `deviceToken` in the payload**. So Okta isn't doing heavy client fingerprinting here; its "attack" is server-side ThreatInsight (IP reputation, velocity, new-location), which is exactly why the MA residential IP and not hammering the account matter.
- **No third-party fingerprinters.** No DataDome / PerimeterX / Imperva / fpjs beacons at all. Assurant is Cloudflare + Okta only.

## Bottom line
Against Assurant we now present a coherent residential Windows browser, Cloudflare passes us silently, Okta sees a familiar-region login with no rich fingerprint to flag. ## 1. Remaining realistic detection/block vectors, ranked

**#1 — Okta server-side risk engine (ThreatInsight + velocity). Already realized, by far the highest.** You measured it: `/api/v1/authn` carries no client fingerprint, so Okta cannot score you on client tells. It scores you on IP reputation, login velocity, and new-location/impossible-travel. Two things in your own setup feed this:
- A **Comcast residential IP** is good for reputation, but Browserbase residential pools are shared. If another tenant burned that exact IP against the same Okta tenant, you inherit the velocity.
- Your flow does **redundant logins**. `login()` calls `prepare()` if not pre-warmed, and the pre-warm path means a `/prepare` + a `/login` can both land. More importantly, any retry, any failed-MFA loop, any double-submit from the frontend = another `POST /api/v1/authn` against the same account. The moment Allstate flips enforcement on those API routes (a config change, not a code deploy), every doc fetch 403s. Note this is **Akamai on Allstate**, distinct from the Cloudflare you measured on Assurant. You have no Akamai sensor execution at all, so you'd fail instantly. Higher likelihood than people think because it's a one-flag change on their side.

**#3 — Cloudflare re-scoring on the in-page API fetches.** Cloudflare passed your *navigation* silently and set `__cf_bm`. But `__cf_bm` is short-lived (~30 min) and scoped, and Bot Management re-scores per request. Your doc fetches are same-origin XHR carrying `__cf_bm`, so they'll usually inherit the pass. The risk is request *shape*: a burst of `/api/Policies/.../Document` calls with a replayed Bearer [REDACTED] a different behavioral signature than the Angular app's natural cadence. Low now (you're passing), but it's the thing most likely to change if you scale runs.

**#4 — Behavioral / timing cadence at the app's own logging layer.** Not a bot vendor, the carrier's own analytics. See Q4.

**#5 — Client JS fingerprint.** Lowest. You measured no third-party fingerprinters, Cloudflare passed, Okta doesn't collect it. The viewport fix was correct but marginal. See Q3.

## 2. Mitigating the Okta velocity/lockout (the real risk)

This is your highest-value work. Concrete:

- **Collapse to exactly one `authn` per user-initiated pull.** Right now `prepare()` and `login()` can both run, and the frontend can double-fire. The busy-lock protects one session; it does **not** stop two sessions hitting the same Okta account.

## The headline: our real risk isn't fingerprint, it's Okta velocity

## It recommends REVERSING the override you just had me add

So we traded a coherent-but-cloudish `2` for a *provably spoofed* accessor, and we measured that **nobody reads this value** (Cloudflare passed, Okta collects none). Net negative.
- **Keep the viewport fix** (that's `browserSettings.viewport`, applied below JS, not a detectable getter).
- **Drop the `hardwareConcurrency`/`deviceMemory` JS override.** ~zero latency, near-zero risk.

`Promise.all` + 700ms jitter still looks like full-list enumeration in the carrier's own server logs (humans click PDFs seconds apart, rarely all of them). assistant
Two reasons, and honestly the second one means my "fix" last turn was misguided:

1. **Verified ignores it, so it does nothing.** The Browserbase docs are explicit: viewport customization is *not available* for Verified sessions, "regardless of the selected OS." So our `viewport: 1920×1080` is a **no-op**, the session still runs the Verified profile's 1280×720. (We haven't re-observed this since you said no more probing, but that's what the docs state, and it lines up: the fp-attack probe showed 1280×720 even though Verified was on.)

2. **The 1280×720 isn't actually a tell on Verified, and overriding it is counterproductive.** This is where I was wrong earlier. I flagged "RTX 3060 Ti + 1280×720 = implausible", true in the abstract, but Verified ships a *coherent, complete* fingerprint profile that Browserbase's bot-protection partners (Cloudflare/Akamai) recognize as legitimate. The 1280×720 is the real value that matches the rest of that profile. The docs explicitly warn: "Avoid modifying viewport dimensions or user agent strings... the pre-made fingerprints are carefully crafted to work together as complete profiles." So forcing a different viewport doesn't make us *more* human, it risks an internal mismatch and can *lower* Verified's success rate.

So it's the exact same lesson as the hw override: on a "real fingerprint" browser, anything we layer on top is either ignored or a new inconsistency. Trust the coherent profile.

To be clear about what I got wrong: flagging 1280×720 as a tell was a reasonable instinct *for a normal session*, but on Verified it's a feature, not a bug. Removing the setting just reverts a no-op and removes the implication that we "fixed" something.

If the small viewport genuinely bothers you, the right lever is `os: "windows"`/`"mac"` (pick a Verified profile whose native resolution you prefer), not a manual viewport override. Want me to remove the viewport setting, or leave it (it's harmless-but-useless) and instead set `os`?

assistant
Done, deviceToken implemented (Assurant/Okta only), typecheck + 32 tests green.

Now your question, and I want to be precise rather than just reassuring:

## Cloudflare (Assurant): solid, with two honest caveats
- **Measured:** Cloudflare passed us **silently**, set `__cf_bm`, no challenge, no Turnstile, no `cf_clearance` needed. The research confirmed that's the *best possible* outcome: it means the ML engine scored us in the human range. With Verified (real partner-recognized fingerprint) + residential IP + real Chrome TLS, the fingerprint approach is structurally sound. So yes, solid.
- **Caveat 1 (coverage):** we only confirmed the **login-page navigation** passing. The probe died at MFA, so we never observed the **doc-fetch phase** (the in-page API burst) being scored by Cloudflare. Those calls carry `__cf_bm` and are same-origin XHR so they *should* inherit the pass, but we haven't watched it end-to-end. The pentester rated this a low-but-real risk.
- **Caveat 2 (point-in-time):** Cloudflare re-scores per request, and whether a given score gets challenged is the *site operator's* WAF rule, which they can change. "Solid" = solid under current conditions, not a permanent guarantee.

## Okta velocity: yes, that's what's actually been throttling us
All three sources agree, and we lived it (today's "try again later" lockout). Okta sends **no client fingerprint**, it scores server-side on IP reputation, **login velocity**, and new-device/location. That's the realized, dominant risk, not Cloudflare. The two fixes target exactly it: **deviceToken** (just shipped, makes Okta recognize the device) and **Contexts** (next, reuse the session so we stop re-logging-in every run).

## One thing not to conflate
"Cloudflare solid" is **Assurant only**. **Allstate is behind Akamai**, a different vendor, and there our `_abck=-1` (unvalidated session) is a separate **latent** risk: it works now only because those API endpoints aren't currently enforcing. That's the one I'd flag as "not solid, just not-yet-enforced," distinct from the Cloudflare picture.

So: Cloudflare/Assurant fingerprint = solid (measured, with the two caveats); Okta velocity = the active throttle we're now fixing; Allstate/Akamai = the latent one to keep an eye on. The only way to upgrade Cloudflare from "solid per the login observation" to "confirmed end-to-end" is one clean full run watching the doc phase, which needs a probe you've paused. Want to leave it paused, or do one clean confirercation run later?

Real data, and it surfaced a problem with the deviceToken I just added. Two findings:

1. **The deviceToken injection broke login, Okta returned `400`.** Before this change, the `authn` POST returned 200→MFA; now with the `page.route` body-rewrite it 400s ("bad request"). The captured body (what the page produced) shows no `context`, and `route.continue({postData})` rewrites it just before send, so the rewrite is almost certainly producing a body Okta rejects (likely a content-length/encoding mismatch through the CDP proxy, or Okta refusing the modified shape). deviceToken is an *optional* risk-trender, not worth breaking login. **Reverting it now.**

2. **Fingerprint is the clean Verified profile** (as intended after removing the overrides): Windows 10, RTX 3060 Ti, `hardwareConcurrency: 2` (Verified's real value, back from our fake 8), deviceMemory 8, 1280×720, NY timezone. So the "trust Verified" state is confirmed, no spoof artifacts.

assistant
Big result, and it overturns the pentester's #1 concern. **`_abck` is VALIDATED (`status=0`), from the very first load**, not `-1`.

## What the probe showed

**Where Akamai fingerprints us (the sensor):**
- `GET /cwnbKR/<obfuscated>` — loads Akamai's obfuscated sensor JS.
- `POST /cwnbKR/<obfuscated>` — exfiltrates the encrypted **sensor_data** (device + behavioral fingerprint), we saw 4 POSTs of 1.9KB → 2.2KB → 4KB as it collected more signal.
- `POST /akam/13/pixel_*` — a second sensor payload (3.6KB).
- F5 BIG-IP cookies (`BIGipServer...`) = load-balancer session affinity, **not** fingerprinting. `conacc.allstate.com` = IBM Security Access Manager (the auth backend). The weird `data.allstate.com/529001/...bank-name...` paths are Akamai telemetry/decoys.

**The verdict, and it's the headline:**
```
_abck status=0   (-1 = UNVALIDATED, 0 = validated)   ← validated on initial load, stayed 0 throughout
cwnbKR POSTs → 201 Created, x-akamai-transformed: 0 - 0 -   (the "0" = accepted; we used to see "l")
```
Akamai **accepted our sensor data and validated the session.** This is the opposite of what we measured before the hardening (`_abck=-1`). **Verified (real Windows Chrome fingerprint Akamai's partners recognize) + the MA residential IP + our real trusted Playwright interactions = the sensor passes.** We're genuinely passing Akamai now, not just riding endpoints that happen to be unenforced.

**Bonus:** the login reached MFA cleanly this run (`outcome: mfa`, `/anon/verification`), Allstate recovered from this morning's throttle, and the validated `_abck` + clean fingerprint sailed through.

## What this means
The pentester's **CRITICAL-1 ("`_abck=-1` is a latent kill switch")** is **resolved** under the hardened config. Our full-API Allstate fetch now rides a *validated* Akamai session, far more robust than the earlier "works only because endpoints aren't enforcing" situation.

Our docs are now stale, `latency.md`/`CLAUDE.md` still say `_abck` stays `-1`. Want me to update them to reflect that the hardened config validates Akamai (`_abck=0`), and note the sensor endpoints we mapped? That's the accurate, and much stronger, story now.
