## It worked end-to-end on prod
Login → MFA → **4 documents pulled** via the hardened in-page path, on the residential IP with Akamai validated. So the hardened approach is confirmed working end-to-end (the thing we hadn't verified).

## But the graded latency is over budget: **10.45s**
```
GRADED mfa-submit->docs: 10450ms
├─ submitMfa (verify code → /secured/ nav): ~3.7s   (Allstate-side, mostly unavoidable)
└─ fetchDocuments: ~6.75s
   ├─ api-list: 3.08s   (GetUserData 2.83s ∥ primer 2.44s — parallel; list 0.20s)
   └─ api-docs: 3.67s   (4 PDFs parallel + jitter, gated by the slowest)
        Renters Policy (1.24MB): 3.21s   ← the big one dominates
        Privacy Statement: 3.65s, New Business: 3.06s / 0.80s
```

## Why it's slower than the old ~5.3s
That ~5.3s was **`page.request` (datacenter IP), fetchDocuments-only**. We deliberately moved to **in-page `fetch`** for anti-bot correctness (residential IP + validated Akamai), and that costs latency: each call is a `page.evaluate` CDP round-trip, and the PDF bytes get base64-encoded *in the browser* and shipped back over CDP. The 1.24MB Renters doc alone is 3.2s of that. This is the **stealth ↔ latency tradeoff made concrete**: we bought anti-bot legitimacy at roughly 2× the doc-fetch cost, and Allstate's heavier doc set (4 docs, one big) pushes the total past 8s.

## The decision
We can't have all three at once here: full anti-bot hardening + Allstate's 4-doc set + sub-8s. Options:
1. **Accept it for Allstate** — correctness over the 8s for this carrier; note the tradeoff in the writeup. (Assurant, 1-2 small docs, would likely still be under 8s on this same path.)
2. **Optimize the in-page binary transfer** — the base64-over-CDP of the 1.24MB PDF is the single biggest cost; a more efficient transfer (or fetching only the docs the user asked for, not all 4) could claw back 1-2s.
3. **Hybrid/UI fallback** for Allstate if 8s is hard-required, but those have their own costs.

My read: this is a legitimate engineering tradeoff to *document*, not a bug. The honest story, "we hit sub-8s with the fast path, but full anti-bot hardening costs ~2× on doc bytes, so the production-safe config is ~10s for a 4-doc Allstate pull", is a strong FDE answer. Want to optimize the transfer (option 2), or accept + document the tradeoff?


user
hmmmm i think for the demo were gonna have to cut it below 8 seconds, thats a strong requirment, we can document the tradeoff, but whats the safest way to get it back to the 5 second


assistant
The honest answer: [REDACTED] `fetch` can't get to 5s**, riding the residential proxy + the `page.evaluate`/base64-over-CDP overhead is inherently ~2× slower per call, and that's the floor. To hit 5s you need `page.request` (Node, datacenter, direct) for the byte-fetch. The good news is that's now **much safer than it was**, here's why, and that's the recommendation.

## The key unlock: Akamai validates the *session*, not the request
We proved `_abck=0` (validated) this morning. Akamai gates `/api/secured` on the **session** (the validated `_abck` cookie + CSRF), not on per-request IP, and `page.request` carries the browser context's cookies (including the validated `_abck`). We even confirmed those endpoints return 200 via `page.request` back when `_abck` was *-1*; on a *validated* session it's strictly safer.

So the architecture that's both fast and safe:
1. **Login stays residential + Verified** → Akamai fingerprints the browser and validates `_abck=0`. This is the part Akamai actually scores, and it's unchanged.
2. **Fetch the docs via `page.request`** (datacenter, fast) carrying that validated session.

## Expected latency
- `page.request` doc-fetch ≈ 3s (and on Fly `iad`, *closer* to Allstate than my local box was, so likely faster than the old 5.3s).
- **No-MFA path (Allstate's common trusted-device case): ~5s** ✓
- **MFA path: ~6.7s** (the ~3.7s MFA verify is carrier-side and unavoidable) ✓ still under 8s.
- (For contrast, in-page MFA path was the 10.45s failure.)

## The split I'd recommend
- **Allstate → revert doc-fetch to `page.request`.** Akamai validates the session, so the fast path rides legitimate cookies. Hits ~5s.
- **Assurant → keep in-page `fetch`.** Cloudflare re-scores *per request* (not a session-cookie gate like Akamai), so datacenter `page.request` there is riskier, and Assurant's docs are small (no 1.24MB monster), so in-page is already fast enough.

## The tradeoff to document (you said that's fine)
Allstate's byte-fetch egresses our datacenter IP / Node TLS, *but on an Akamai-validated session*. `allstate-ui-fallback` stays as the escape hatch if it's ever walled.

This keeps the heavy anti-bot work (residential Verified login, validated Akamai) and just makes the byte transfer fast. Want me to revert Allstate's `fetchDocuments` to `page.request`, redeploy, and you re-test from the UI?

`page.request` did its job on the bytes, the 1.24MB doc dropped from 3.2s → 2.0s. And Akamai still passed (the calls returned 200 on the validated session). So **fast + safe** worked.

## Two honest caveats
1. **The 10.45s → 5.92s headline is partly because this run skipped MFA.** Apples-to-apples, `page.request` saved ~0.8s on `fetchDocuments` itself (5.9s vs 6.75s in-page). The big swing is MFA-or-not.
2. **If MFA *does* fire, it's ~9.5s** (submitMfa ~3.7s, carrier-side + fetchDocuments ~5.9s), over 8s. Allstate usually skips MFA on a trusted device (as it did here), so the demo will *likely* show ~5.9s, but it's not guaranteed every run.

## To get real margin / reliably hit ~5s even with MFA
The remaining lever is **`api-list` (3.43s)** — `GetUserData` and the primer are small JSON calls taking ~2.2-2.6s each (likely per-call TLS handshake with no connection reuse). That's the slowest reducible piece now. It's data-dependent (policy# + primer must finish before the list), so options are limited, but worth a look if you want margin.

My take: **5.92s on the common no-MFA path is a solid demo number and meets the 8s requirement.** Want to leave it here and document the MFA-path caveat, or should I dig into trimming `api-list` for more headroom?


user
why is the fetch documents taking 5.9 seconds tho?


assistant
Good question, and the answer is mostly **Allstate's server**, not our code. Here's the breakdown:

```
fetchDocuments 5.92s = three data-dependent phases that CAN'T overlap:
  Phase 1  [GetUserData 2.23s  ∥  primer 2.62s]   → 2.62s  (parallel, gated by primer)
  Phase 2   GetPolicySpecificDocsList             → 0.79s
  Phase 3  [4 × GetUdpRetrieveDocument ∥ + jitter] → 2.49s  (parallel, gated by slowest + jitter)
                                                     ─────
                                                     ~5.9s
```

**The phases are sequential because of data dependencies:** you need the policy number (GetUserData) + the primed context before the list call works; you need the contentIds from the list before you can fetch any doc. So the floor is `phase1 + phase2 + phase3`, even though *within* each phase we already run everything in parallel.

**Why each small call is ~2.5s = it's Allstate's API, not our transport.** GetUserData and the primer return tiny JSON, yet take 2.2-2.6s each. The proof: those same two calls were **2.83s / 2.44s in the in-page run and 2.23s / 2.62s with page.request**, basically identical regardless of how we fetch. If it were our overhead, page.request would've been much faster. It wasn't, because the time is Allstate's backend (Akamai edge → F5 → IBM ISAM session check → their app server). Enterprise insurance APIs are just slow. The *only* thing page.request sped up was the 1.24MB PDF (3.2s → 2.0s), because that one was transport-bound (base64-over-CDP), not server-bound.

So: ~2.6s (phase 1, their server) + 0.8s (list, their server) + ~2.5s (docs, their server + a bit of jitter) = 5.9s, and the bulk is them.

**What's actually trimmable on our side (small):**
- **Jitter:** the docs phase carries up to 700ms of random stagger. Drop it to ~200ms → save ~0.5s.
- **GetUserData:** it's parallel with the primer (the gate), so dropping it (e.g. read the policy number from the page DOM, it's on the dashboard) only saves ~0.4s, since the primer's 2.6s still gates phase 1.

Realistic floor: ~5.0-5.2s. We can't go much below that without removing a required call, because **~4s of the 5.9s is Allstate's server responding**, and the three phases can't be collapsed. Want me to grab the easy ~0.5-0.9s (trim jitter + DOM policy number), or leave it at 5.9s?

assistant
You've caught a real problem, and you're right: **9.4-9.5s is the true number, and my 5.9s was measuring too narrow a span.**

Here's the reconciliation. On the no-MFA path, your `/login` request does the **whole** thing, and the `GRADED login->docs` log I quoted starts its clock *after* `login()` finishes, so it only timed `fetchDocuments` (5.9s). But the user clicks "login" and waits for `login()` **plus** `fetchDocuments`:

```
UI submit → on screen  ≈ 9.5s
├─ login()              ~3.2s   (form-actions 0.2s + auth-nav 3.0s = Allstate authenticating + redirect)
├─ fetchDocuments        5.9s   (api-list 3.4s + api-docs 2.5s)
└─ network + render     ~0.3s
```

So the 5.9s was the fetch only; the auth step (~3.2s, which the user absolutely waits through) wasn't in it. The spec says "login → documents," that's the 9.4s, and we're **over 8s**.

## The hard truth
About **~7s of the 9.4s is Allstate's own backend**, not our code:
- `auth-nav` 3.0s = Allstate verifying credentials → `/secured/` redirect (through Akamai + F5 + IBM ISAM).
- `api-list` 3.4s + part of `api-docs` = their slow `/api/secured` endpoints (~2.5s per small JSON call, confirmed server-bound).

Only ~2s is ours-and-parallelizable, and it already is parallelized. Allstate's stack is just slow.

## What's actually trimmable
- Jitter (up to 0.7s) → drop to ~0.2s: **~0.5s**
- Policy number from the page DOM instead of `GetUserData`: **~0.4s**
- A faster post-MFA/auth wait signal instead of full nav: maybe **~0.3s**

Best realistic case ≈ **~8.2-8.5s**. Getting *reliably* under 8s on Allstate is genuinely hard because their server is the floor.

## Honest options
1. **Trim the ~1s and accept ~8.5s for Allstate**, document that Allstate's backend is the constraint, and lean on **Assurant as the under-8s carrier** (small docs, no 3s auth-nav, likely ~5-6s). The "~8s" in the spec has a tilde.
2. **Re-measure honestly** and present 9.4s with the breakdown showing 7s is the carrier, that's a legitimate FDE answer ("here's exactly where the time goes and what's us vs them").
3. Push harder on auth-nav, but that's Allstate's server; limited upside.

I'd correct the `GRADED` log to span the full `login→docs` (so we stop fooling ourselves), grab the ~1s of trims, and frame Allstate as carrier-bound at ~8.5s with Assurant as the fast path. Want me to do that?

Full picture captured. And it surfaced a **big, fixable lever**. The perf-engineer is still analyzing, but here's the complete breakdown:

## login → docs (no-MFA): **9.65s** (matches your UI 9.4-9.5s ✓)
```
prepare() [PRE-WARM, off the graded clock]: 11.4s   (form-hydrate 5.7s is the bulk)
login():        3.42s   (form-actions 0.84s + auth-nav 2.58s = Allstate auth)
fetchDocuments: 6.24s   (api-list 3.01s + api-docs 3.23s)
─────────────────────────────────────────────────
GRADED login→docs: 9.65s
```

## The discovery: the API calls are slow because they're COLD/CONTENDED, not because the server is slow
The micro-benchmark is the smoking gun:
```
warm GetUserData (page.request, connection warm): 359ms / 391ms / 400ms   (~0.4s)
cold GetUserData (during fetchDocuments):         ~2.2s
```
**Same endpoint, 0.4s warm vs 2.2s cold → ~1.8s per call is recoverable.** Allstate's server isn't inherently 2s; warm it's 0.4s.

Two causes, both visible in the waterfall:
1. **`page.request` opens its own cold connection**, separate from the browser's (which warmed up during login). First calls pay setup.
2. **The SPA floods the backend right as we fetch.** After login, Allstate's dashboard fires a pile of slow `/api/secured` calls *concurrently* with ours, all TTFB-dominated (server time):
   - `AlliancePolicyData` **2.9s**, `GetUserData` 2.0s, `GetUserCards` 1.6s, `GetMyHomeAddress` 0.85s, `GetPersonalizedUserData`, `GetUserIdentity` 0.78s.
   
   Our calls contend with that flood on Allstate's backend → slow. Once it settles (the microbench), the server responds in 0.4s.

## What this means
This is the opposite of "Allstate's server is the floor." It's **~0.4s warm**, so the ~1.8s/call penalty is from cold connections + dashboard contention, both addressable. If we pre-warm `page.request`'s connection and/or dodge the dashboard flood (e.g., wait a beat for it to drain, or warm the connection during the auth wait), `api-list` could go ~3.0s → ~1s and `api-docs` similar, plausibly taking fetchDocuments ~6.2s → ~3s and **login→docs under ~7s**.

The perf-engineer is turning this into a concrete, ranked plan (connection pre-warm, contention avoidance, possible in-page-for-JSON/page.request-for-bytes split). I'll fold its recommendations in the moment it lands, then we pick the changes.

## 1. TLS pre-warm during prepare() — expected 800-1,400ms, low risk

Your hypothesis is correct and is likely the dominant remaining lever on the API path.

`page.request` is Playwright's built-in `APIRequestContext`. Under the hood it is Node's `undici` HTTP client (since Playwright 1.38). `undici` maintains a connection pool keyed by origin. When you fire `GetUserData` and the primer in parallel (line 124), they are the FIRST requests from this `APIRequestContext` instance to `myaccountrwd.allstate.com`. Both hit a cold pool simultaneously, so each opens its own TCP+TLS connection rather than one opening and the other waiting to multiplex. That explains why two parallel calls that would be fast on a warm connection each take 2.2-2.6s. The third call (GetPolicySpecificDocsList at 0.79s) reuses one of those warmed connections. The doc calls (0.4-2.0s) are also warm. The TLS handshake to an Akamai-fronted origin from Fly iad is plausibly 200-400ms on its own. The cold-start penalty you are seeing on the first parallel burst (vs the warm sequential calls) is the connection setup replicated across both concurrent sockets plus whatever slow-start applies to the first request on each TCP stream.

How to pre-warm during `prepare()`. The simplest approach is a throwaway authenticated HEAD or GET to `${origin}/api/secured/GetUserData` at the end of `prepare()`, after the login form loads but before `login()` is called. This will not succeed (no auth cookies yet), and you do not need it to. What you need is for the TCP+TLS connection to `myaccountrwd.allstate.com` to be established and sitting idle in `undici`'s pool by the time `fetchDocuments()` runs. A GET that returns 401/403 still completes the TLS handshake and leaves a keep-alive connection open.

There is a catch: `page.request` creates a new `APIRequestContext` per page context, scoped to the browser context's lifetime. The instance in `fetchDocuments()` is the same `page.request` that exists during `prepare()`, because it is the same page object (`this.session.page`). So a call made in `prepare()` using `page.request` writes to the same connection pool that `fetchDocuments()` reads from. Keep-
… [truncated 10829 chars]

**Top 2 fixes (low-risk) get us to ~7.7s:**

My microbench proved the ceiling: warm GetUserData = **0.4s** vs cold 2.2s. **Expected ~1.2-1.5s.**

Combined: `api-list` 3.4s → ~1.7s, → **~7.7s login→docs, under 8s.** Item 3 (fire API calls the moment auth cookies appear, overlapping auth-nav) could shave another ~0.4s but is medium-risk (CSRF timing race), so defer.

Now I've got it. Looking at the logs + the frontend, here's the hypothesis, and it's well-supported.

## Hypothesis: the 22s is a pre-warm MISS, not a slow fetch
The server's doc fetch was actually **fine, 6.9s** (and the pre-warm worked: `api-list` dropped to 2.7s). The extra ~15s is a **second, full `prepare()` running inside `/login`**:
- Logs show **two `session-create`s** (16:06:43 and :51) and **two `form-hydrate`s** (~5-6s each).
- The frontend fires `/prepare` on the carrier `change` event and only sets `warmId` *when `/prepare` returns*. But `prepare()` takes **~11s** (session-create 0.5 + cdp 1 + goto 3 + form-hydrate ~6). 
- You submitted login **before `/prepare` finished**, so `warmId` was still `null`. Backend `/login` with no warmId → builds a fresh session → `login()` calls `prepare()` internally → full ~11s prepare **inside the request**.
- ~11s prepare + 3.6s login + 6.9s fetch ≈ **21.5s ≈ your 22.4s**. ✓

**It's not my pre-warm/jitter commit.** The pre-warm GET is non-awaited, so it doesn't delay when `prepare()` returns; and the fetch was actually faster this run. This is a pre-existing race: `prepare()` is slower (~11s) than the time it takes you to type creds, so the warm session often isn't ready, and `/login` silently falls back to a cold prepare.

## Replicating it before any patch
I want to confirm the mechanism two ways:
1. **Cheap / deterministic (no Allstate login):** drive the prod endpoints with the **mock** carrier, `/prepare` then `/login` *without* `warmId` vs *with* `warmId`, and confirm from the logs that the no-warmId call spins up a second session (proves the reuse-miss path in `app.ts`). This proves the *logic* without burning a real login.
2. **Real (1 Allstate login):** script the exact fast-user race against prod, POST `/prepare` and immediately POST `/login` with `warmId:null`, and watch it reproduce ~22s + two sessions.

## One thing this also exposed (separate, harder)
Even on the **warm** path, login→docs is ~9.4s (login 3.6s + fetch ~6s), still over 8s, because Allstate's auth-nav (3s) + their API (~6s) is ~9s of *their* server. So there are really two problems: the **22s race** (this one, fixable in the frontend/handoff) and the **~9.4s warm baseline** (the genuinely hard one).

Want me to run replication #1 (mock, cheap) to confirm the reuse-miss, then we design the fix? Or go straight to #2 with a real login?


user
ok do the first cheap replication just to prove our hyptothesis right now.


assistant
On it. Cheap replication via the mock carrier (no Browserbase, no Allstate), proving the `app.ts` reuse logic: with `warmId` → reuses the prewarmed session; without `warmId` → builds a fresh one (= the cold `prepare()` path). Let me check the mock's login contract first.

import type { Carrier, Document } from "../types.js";
import { validateDocuments } from "../documents.js";
import { InvalidCredentialsError, InvalidMfaError, DocumentsUnavailableError } from "../errors.js";

// Fake carrier for building and testing the full flow without a real portal.
// It can't read a page, so it picks the outcome from the username:
//   "baduser" -> bad credentials
//   "nomfa"   -> logs in with no MFA step
//   "docfail" -> logs in with no MFA, then fails fetching documents
//   anything else -> needs MFA, accepts code "123456"
const VALID_CODE = "123456";

const SAMPLE_PDF = "JVBERi0xLjAKJcK1"; // tiny base64 stub, stands in for a real PDF

export class MockCarrier implements Carrier {
    readonly name = "mock";
    private loggedIn = false;
    private failDocs = false;

    async prepare(): Promise<void> {
        // no real browser to warm
    }

    async login(username: string, _password: [REDACTED] Promise<{ mfaRequired: boolean }> {
        if (username === "baduser") throw new InvalidCredentialsError("bad username or password");
        if (username === "docfail") {
            this.loggedIn = true;
            this.failDocs = true;
            return { mfaRequired: false };
        }
        if (username === "nomfa") {
            this.loggedIn = true;
            return { mfaRequired: false };
        }
        return { mfaRequired: true };
    }

    async submitMfa(code: string): Promise<void> {
        if (code !== VALID_CODE) throw new InvalidMfaError("wrong or expired code");
        this.loggedIn = true;
    }

    async fetchDocuments(): Promise<Document[]> {
        if (this.failDocs) throw new DocumentsUnavailableError("simulated document fetch failure");
        const docs: Document[] = [
            { name: "declarations.pdf", contentType: "application/pdf", bytes: SAMPLE_PDF },
        ];
        return validateDocuments(this.name, docs);
    }

    async close(): Promise<void> {
        this.loggedIn = false;
    }
}

assistant
**Hypothesis confirmed.** The mock replication proves the exact mechanism:

```
A (no warmId):   /prepare → d5f9d2e2…   /login → ecedf7c3…   reused? FALSE  ← fresh session (cold prepare path)
B (with warmId): /prepare → e4dd36fa…   /login → e4dd36fa…   reused? TRUE   ← reuses the prewarmed session
```

So: **`/login` without a `warmId` builds a brand-new session** (different sessionId), which on the real Allstate carrier means `login()` runs the full `prepare()` inline. With a `warmId`, it reuses the prewarmed one. That's the whole 22s:

> user submits before `/prepare` (~11s) returns its `warmId` → frontend sends `warmId: null` → `/login` cold-prepares (~11s) + login (3.6s) + fetch (6.9s) ≈ 22s.

Root cause nailed, and it's **not** the pre-warm/jitter commit, it's the prewarm handoff racing the user. The fix lives in the prepare→login handoff, not the doc fetch.

Fix options (for when you're ready):
1. **Frontend:** fire `/prepare` on page load (not just on carrier `change`) so it starts ASAP, and **gate the login button** (or queue the submit) until `warmId` is set, so a cold `/login` can't happen.
2. **Backend:** if `/login` arrives with no `warmId` but a prepare is in flight, wait for it instead of starting a second cold prepare.

Option 1 is simpler and removes the race entirely. Want me to do it?


user
ok lets go with option 1

Looked, and good news hiding in a bad run: **the fix worked, and the doc fetch is now genuinely fast.** This run (16:18) shows:
```
ONE prepare (no double):  session-create 428ms, goto 2.5s, form-hydrate 4.6s
form-actions:    409ms
submit+detect:  10109ms   ← THE SLOWNESS: Allstate's auth-nav took 10s
api-list:        1119ms   ← pre-warm working beautifully (was 2.7-3.4s)
api-docs:        2769ms
GRADED fetchDocuments:  3897ms   ← 3.9s, well under 8s
```

So:
- **The 22s race is dead** — single session, single prepare. Fix confirmed.
- **fetchDocuments dropped to 3.9s** (api-list 1.1s, the TLS pre-warm landed perfectly).
- **The "mega slow" was Allstate's authentication: `auth-nav` 10.1s.** That's Allstate's auth server being slow *this run*, the identical code the prior run did auth-nav in 3.2s. Pure carrier-side variance (it swings 3-10s), nothing in our code.

So submit→docs = auth-nav (Allstate, **variable 3-10s**) + fetch (3.9s):
- Good auth run (~3s): **~7s, under 8s** ✓
- Bad auth run (10s, like this one): ~14s ✗

The piece we control (fetch) is now ~3.9s. The wildcard is Allstate's auth latency, which we can't touch and which is wildly variable.

Two honest implications:
1. **Re-run it, you'll likely see ~7s** on a normal auth spell, this was a bad one.
2. Allstate **can** dip under 8s but won't *reliably*, because their auth swings to 10s. **Assurant** is the dependable sub-8s carrier.

Want to re-run Allstate a couple times to see the spread (I'll grab each from logs), or move on and lock Assurant as the sub-8s story?

assistant
Okay, I looked carefully, and it's time I gave you the straight answer instead of another "one more fix." This run was actually **clean** (single prepare, no double, fix working), but it exposes the real floor.

This run's server timing:
```
ONE prepare (good): session-create 0.5s, goto 3.0s, form-hydrate 4.8s  (~9s total)
login (submit+detect):  3.99s   (auth-nav 3.6s — normal)
fetchDocuments:         7.86s   (api-list 2.7s, api-docs 5.2s ← spiked this run)
```
Server `/login` work = login 4.0s + fetch 7.9s = **11.8s**. You saw 17.4s. The extra ~5.6s is the **`await preparing` wait**: you hard-refreshed and submitted *fast* (pasted creds), before the ~9s prepare finished, so login waited for it. That's the tradeoff I made, it killed the 22s double-prepare, but if you submit before prepare is done, you wait for it. A real user typing email+password (~10-15s) wouldn't hit that; fast-paste testing does.

## The honest floor for Allstate
Even with everything optimized and prepare fully warm, **submit→docs = login (~4s) + fetch (~5-8s) ≈ 9-12s**, and ~7s of that is **Allstate's own servers**:
- ISAM auth: ~3.5s (spikes to 10s)
- their `/api/secured` PDF generation: 5-8s, and it's *variable* (2.6s one run, 5.2s the next)

I optimized the parts we own (pre-warm cut api-list to ~1s; killed the double-prepare). But **Allstate cannot reliably hit sub-8s from submit**, the auth + their PDF API floor is ~9s, and it swings higher. No client change fixes their backend. That's not me giving up; it's what every run's data says.

## What I'd actually do
1. **Make Assurant the sub-8s demo carrier.** Smaller docs, no 3.5s ISAM auth, it's the one that genuinely lands under 8s. Lead the Loom with Assurant for the latency requirement.
2. **Frame Allstate honestly** with this exact breakdown: "doc fetch optimized to ~1s api-list via TLS pre-warm; the residual is Allstate's ISAM auth + PDF API, ~7s of their backend." That's a *strong* FDE answer, it shows you measured precisely where the time goes and what's yours vs theirs.
3. Optional ~1s: the perf-engineer's ISAM-204 auth speedup (resolve the moment cookies are set, start fetch in parallel). Real but marginal; won't change the "Allstate ~9s" reality.

I don't think more Allstate tuning is worth your time, the data's clear it's their server. Want me to implement the ~1s auth speedup anyway, or pivot to verifying Assurant's sub-8s number and building the demo around that?

Okta /verify:       200  status: SUCCESS, sessionToken issued   ← YOUR MFA WORKED PERFECTLY
captured Bearer:    Bearer <1276>  ✓
session cookies:    DT, sid, __cf_bm  ✓  (fully authed)
doc-list request:   sent Bearer + ui-transaction  ✓
doc-list response:  "Unauthorized"   ← the ONLY failure
```

So:
- **MFA is not broken. Not throttled. Your code [MFA] was accepted (status SUCCESS).** Login, auth, MFA, Bearer [REDACTED], cookies, all correct.
- **The single failure is the pure-API doc-list call returning "Unauthorized" despite a valid Bearer.** Root cause: the doc API (`/api/Policies/.../Documents`) needs the **server-side session context the Angular app establishes during normal navigation** (remember the `POST /api/SetOktaUserIdAndToken` we saw in the first probe). Our pure-API shortcut skips that nav, so the token is valid but the session isn't "registered" for the doc API → Unauthorized.

This is a pure-API-rewrite problem, and we have a **proven version that doesn't have it**: the original **UI-download Assurant navigated the app normally** (so the session got established) and pulled docs at **~7.8s**. Reverting to it fixes this cleanly. Doing it now (code-only revert, no MFA needed):

You're right, and I owe you the blunt version instead of more optimization whack-a-mole: **Allstate cannot reliably hit 8s from the UI, and no change on our side fixes that.** Here's the honest anatomy of that 17s:

```
~5-9s   waiting for pre-warm   ← you submit before the ~9s prepare finishes; login() awaits it
~4s     login (Allstate ISAM auth)   ← their server, swings 3-10s
~4-8s   fetchDocuments               ← their /api, swings (one run api-docs spiked to 5.2s)
```

Two of those three are **Allstate's own backend** (~7-12s of pure server time). The doc fetch we already optimized as far as it goes. So the floor for Allstate "submit → docs" is **~8-12s**, period. I can shave ~1-2s but I cannot make Allstate 8s, and I'd be lying if I said otherwise.

The first chunk (the pre-warm wait) is the one that's partly an artifact: the `await preparing` makes you wait out the prewarm if you submit fast. A real user typing email+password (~10-15s) wouldn't see it, the prewarm finishes during typing. Your paste-and-submit testing eats it.

## What I can actually do (helps the demo, won't make Allstate 8s)
1. **"Ready" indicator** — disable the login button / show "Preparing…→Ready" until the prewarm completes. Then you submit *warm*, removing the ~5-9s wait from the perceived number → Allstate demos at ~9-11s instead of 17s. Clean for the Loom. (5-min frontend change.)
2. **ISAM-204 login speedup** (~1s) — resolve auth the instant Allstate's auth POST returns, start fetch in parallel. Marginal.

33	            // Verified (Scale plan): purpose-built Chromium with REAL fingerprints
34	            // recognized as legitimate by Browserbase's bot-protection partners

assistant
**Found the actual bug, and it's a simple one.** Look at the screenshot: the page **did** reach the documents page, the "Policy Documents" heading is right there, the Confirmation of Coverage card is loading. But the carrier's check is `waitForURL(/\/Policy\/Documents/)`, and Assurant's SPA renders that view **without the URL matching that pattern**, so `waitForURL` times out at 30s and throws "nav-docs failed", even though the page loaded fine.

The fix: wait for the **heading** (which the screenshot proves renders), not the URL. Surgical change:

Saved as `working-demo` tag (instant restore point). Here's where the 15.5s goes:

```
submitMfa (Okta verify + nav):  ~2.5s
nav-docs (selection→snapshot→docs page): 5.9s
trigger-downloads:  4.2s  ┐
retrieve-list:      2.2s  ├─ ~7s = the download-to-Browserbase-storage mechanism (the replaceable part)
fetch-bytes:        0.6s  ┘
```

The big, clear lever: that **~7s of download-to-storage** (click each download → Browserbase syncs to cloud → poll Downloads API → fetch bytes). The fix is the hybrid we couldn't do before: now that `nav-docs` lands us **on the documents page (session established)**, we can scrape the doc URLs from the rendered anchors and **in-page `fetch` them directly** (cookie-authed, like the anchor click), skipping the storage round-trip entirely. That's exactly the "establishing nav" the pure-API was missing when it got Unauthorized.

Target: ~7s → ~1-2s, so **15.5s → ~10s**. Let me read the current fetch section and wire it:

name: string }[] = [];\n        for (const a of await page.locator(\"a[href*='documentToken']\").all()) {\n            const href = await a.getAttribute(\"href\");\n            if (!href) continue;\n            const name = (await a.innerText().catch(() => \"\")).trim() || \"Policy document\";\n            targets.push({ url: new URL(href, origin).toString(), name });\n        }\n        // Confirmation of Coverage: build from the FULL policy number in a doc href\n        // (the selection-list shows a shorter form the POI endpoint rejects).\n        const fullRen = targets[0]?.url.match(/Policies\\/(REN\\d+)\\//i)?.[1];\n        if (fullRen)\n            targets.push({ url: `${origin}/api/v2/Policies/${fullRen}/POI/ConfirmationOfCoverage?outputMode=file`, name: \"Confirmation of Coverage\" });\n        if (targets.length === 0)\n            throw new DocumentsUnavailableError(\"no documents available yet (policy may still be processing)\");\n\n        const fetched = await Promise.all(\n            targets.map(async (t) => {\n                const r = await session.fetchInPage(t.url, { headers: { accept: \"application/pdf,*/*\" } });\n                // skip anything not returned as a PDF (e.g. a transient error page),\n                // rather than failing the whole pull on one bad document.\n                if (r.status >= 400 || !/pdf|octet-stream/i.test(r.contentType)) return null;\n                return { name: t.name, contentType: r.contentType, bytes: r.base64 } satisfies Document;\n            }),\n        );\n        const docs = fetched.filter((d): d is Document => d !== null);\n        console.log(`[timing] api-docs (in-page): ${Date.now() - tFetch}ms`);\n        return validateDocuments(this.name, docs);"}
